   <h2>Publication and Citation-based Impact</h2>   <p>I have been collecting data on citations of my own work for a number of years and once wrote a <a href="https://peerj.com/preprints/477/">guide to the concepts for biologists</a> (rather than for those better versed in bibliometrics and scientometrics) (put on <em>PeerJ Preprint Server,</em> 2014-08-26). I have expanded the collection of metrics beyond those described in the paper and have now created a separate webpage for every metric, including a basic explanation, a worked example (in many cases), and a year-by-year history of that metric based on my own publication record.</p>
   <p>The code for calculating all of these metrics can be found on <a href="https://github.com/msrosenberg/ImpactFactor"><span class="fab fa-github"></span> Github</a>.</p>
      <h3>Common Symbols and Definitions</h3>
        <ul>
          <li><em>P</em> &mdash; The total number of publications of an author. Unless otherwise specified, publications are in rank order from 1&hellip;<em>P,</em> with 1 having the most citations and <em>P</em> the fewest.</li>
          <li><em>C<sub>i</sub></em> &mdash; The number of citations for the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication.</li>
          <li><em>C<sup>x</sup></em> &mdash; The sum of citations for the top <em>x</em> publications, \(C^x=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{x}{C_i}\).</li>
          <li><em>A<sub>i</sub></em> &mdash; The number of authors of the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication.</li>
          <li><em>a<sub>i</sub></em> &mdash; The ordered position of the focal author among the full author list of the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication, it's value can range from 1 to <em>A<sub>i</sub>.</em></li>
          <li><em>Y<sub>i</sub></em> &mdash; The year of the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication.</li>
          <li><em>Y</em><sub>0</sub></em> &mdash; The year of the author's first publication, \(Y_0=\min\left(Y_i\right)\).</li>
          <li>academic age &mdash; The number of years since an author's first publication. If <em>Y</em> is the current year (or year of interest), the academic age of the author is \(Y-Y_0+1\).</li>
        </ul>

    <div id="total_pubs" class="metric_container">
      <h2>total publications</h2>
      <p>This metric is simply the total number of publications by an author. Many works might be considered a publication, including journal articles, books, book chapters, published conference abstracts, software, reports, dissertations, and theses.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>P</em></th></tr>

    <div id="c_p" class="metric_container">
      <h2>mean citations per publication</h2>
      <p>This metric is the mean number of citations per publication. It has been described under many names, including the <span class="metric_name"><em>C/P</em> index</span>, the <span class="metric_name">mean citation rate (<em>MCR</em>)</span>, the <span class="metric_name">mean observed citation rate (<em>MOCR</em>)</span>, <span class="metric_name">citations per publication (<em>CPP</em>)</span>, the <span class="metric_name">observed citation rate (<em>OCR</em>)</span>, the <span class="metric_name">generalized impact factor (<em>I<sub>f</sub></em>)</span>, and the <span class="metric_name">journal paper citedness (<em>JPC</em>)</span>.</p>$$C/P\text{ index}=\frac{C^P}{P}$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="pubs_per_year" class="metric_container">
      <h2>publications per year</h2>
      <p>This metric, also called the <span class="metric_name">time-scaled number of publications</span> is just the mean number of publications per year, calculated as the total number of publications of an author divided by their academic age (number of years since their first publication),</p>$$P^{TS}=\frac{P}{\text{academic age}}=\frac{P}{Y-Y_0+1},$$<p>where \(Y\) is the current year and \(Y_{0}\) is the year of their first publication.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">

    <div id="citations_per_year" class="metric_container">
      <h2>citations per year</h2>
      <p>This metric, also called the <span class="metric_name">time-scaled citation index</span>, is just the mean number of citations per year, calculated as the total number of citations of an author divided by their academic age (number of years since their first publication),</p>$$C^{TS}=\frac{C^P}{\text{academic age}}=\frac{C^P}{Y-Y_0+1},$$<p>where \(Y\) is the current year and \(Y_{0}\) is the year of their first publication.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <span class="metric_name"><em>h-</em>index</span> (Hirsch 2005) is the most important personal impact factor one needs to be familiar with, not because it is necessarily the best, but because (1) it was the first major index of its type and most of the other indices are based on it in some way, and (2) it is the single factor with which most other people (<em>e.g.</em>, administrators) are likely to be somewhat familiar. You may find another index which you prefer, but everything starts with <em>h.</em></p><p>The <em>h-</em>index is defined as the largest value for which <em>h</em> publications have at least <em>h</em> citations. Put another way, a scientist has an impact factor of <em>h</em> if <em>h</em> of their publications have at least <em>h</em> citations and the other <em>P - h</em> publications have ≤ <em>h</em> citations. Note that <em>h</em> is measured in publications. In formal notation, one might write</p>$$h=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i\leq C_i\right).$$<p>These top <em>h</em> publications are often referred to as the &ldquo;Hirsch core.&rdquo;</p><div id="chart_h_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>One way to graphically visualize <em>h</em> is to imagine a plot of citation count versus rank for all publications (often called the citation curve). By definition, this plot will generally trend from upper left (highest ranked publications with most citations, to lower right (lowest ranked publications with fewest citations), depending on the precise citation distribution of the author. If one were to add a (threshold) line with a slope of one to this plot, the point where the threshold line crosses the citation curve (truncated to an integer) is <em>h.</em> Alternatively, one can visualize <em>h</em> as the size (length of sides) of the largest square that one can fit under the citation curve.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em>&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 6.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">3</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">6</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">6</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">8</td></tr>

    <div id="h_core_cites" class="metric_container">
      <h2>Hirsch core citations</h2>
      <p>This is the sum of the citations for all publications in the Hirsch core,</p>$$C^H=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{C_i}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">49</td><td class="box">27</td><td class="box">23</td><td class="box">13</td><td class="box">10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The sum of the citations for the top <em>h</em> publications is 132.</p>

    <div id="g_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>g-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The best known and most widely studied alternative to the <em>h-</em>index is known as the <em>g-</em>index (Egghe 2006a, b, c). The <em>g-</em>index is designed to give more credit for publications cited in excess of the <em>h</em> threshold. The primary difference between the formal definitions of the <em>h-</em> and <em>g-</em>indices is that <em>g</em> is based on cumulative citation counts rather than individual citation counts. Formally, the <em>g-</em>index is the largest value for which <em>g</em> publications have jointly received at least <em>g</em><sup>2</sup> citations.</p>$$g=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i^2\leq \sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{C_j}\right)=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i\leq\frac{\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{C_j}}{i} \right)$$<div class="chart2container"><div id="chart_g_index_desc1_div" class="proportional_chart2"></div><div id="chart_g_index_desc2_div" class="proportional_chart2"></div></div><div class="clear_float"><p>Although not usually formulated this way, the above also shows an alternative interpretation of the <em>g-</em>index, which makes it's meaning and relationship to <em>h</em> much clearer: the <em>g-</em>index is the largest value for which the top <em>g</em> publications average <em>g</em> citations, while <em>h</em> is the largest value for which the top <em>h</em> publications have a minimum of <em>h</em> citations.</p><p>Stricly speaking, it is possible for the number of citations in the <em>g-</em>core to exceed the square of the total number of publications (<em>C<sup>P</sup></em> > <em>P</em><sup>2</sup>), or using the alternate definition, for the average number of citations per publication to exceed the number of publications. Under this scenario, the threshold curve and the citation curve do not actually cross. Some authors have suggested adding phantom publications with zero citations until the curves cross (essentially, making <em>g</em> equal to the square-root of <em>C<sup>P</sup></em>); a more conservative approach, illustrated here, is to set the maximum possible value of <em>g</em> equal to the number of publications.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Cumulative Citations (Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>76</td><td>99</td><td>112</td><td>122</td><td>132</td><td>135</td><td>138</td><td>141</td><td>143</td><td>144</td><td class="box">145</td><td>146</td><td>146</td><td>146</td><td>146</td></tr><tr><th>Rank Squared (<em>i</em><sup>2</sup>)</th><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>9</td><td>16</td><td>25</td><td>36</td><td>49</td><td>64</td><td>81</td><td>100</td><td>121</td><td class="box">144</td><td>169</td><td>196</td><td>225</td><td>256</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>g</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;12</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td class="box">12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Mean Citations (Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> / <em>i</em>)</th><td>49.0</td><td>38.0</td><td>33.0</td><td>28.0</td><td>24.4</td><td>22.0</td><td>19.3</td><td>17.2</td><td>15.7</td><td>14.3</td><td>13.1</td><td class="box">12.1</td><td>11.2</td><td>10.4</td><td>9.7</td><td>9.1</td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em><sup>2</sup> ≤ Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> (or <em>i</em> ≤ mean <em>C<sub>i</sub></em>) is 12.</p>

    <div id="Tol_f_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>f-</em>index (Tol)</h2>
      <p>If the <em>h-</em> and <em>g-</em>indices are the largest value <em>x</em> for which <em>x</em> publications have a minimum (<em>h</em>) or mean (<em>g</em>) number of citations equal to <em>x,</em> then Tol's <em>f-</em>index (Tol 2007) is the same idea, except based on the harmonic mean number of citations for the top <em>f</em> publications.</p>$$f=\underset{i}{\max}\left(\frac{1}{\frac{1}{i}\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{\frac{1}{C_j}}}\geq i\right)=\underset{i}{\max}\left(\frac{i}{\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{\frac{1}{C_j}} }\geq i\right)$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td class="box">7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Harmonic Mean Citations</th><td>49.0</td><td>34.8</td><td>29.7</td><td>22.5</td><td>18.0</td><td>15.9</td><td class="box">9.8</td><td>7.7</td><td>6.5</td><td>5.3</td><td>3.8</td><td>3.1</td><td>2.7</td><td>2.9</td><td>3.1</td><td>3.3</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>f</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;7</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em> ≤ harmonic mean citations is 7.</p>

    <div id="Tol_t_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>t-</em>index (Tol)</h2>
      <p>If the <em>h-</em> and <em>g-</em>indices are the largest value <em>x</em> for which <em>x</em> publications have a minimum (<em>h</em>) or mean (<em>g</em>) number of citations equal to <em>x,</em> then Tol's <em>t-</em>index (Tol 2007) is the same idea, except based on the geometric mean number of citations for the top <em>t</em> publications.</p>$$t=\underset{i}{\max}\left(\exp\left(\frac{1}{i}\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}\ln{C_j}\right)\geq i\right)$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td class="box">9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Geometric Mean Citations</th><td>49.0</td><td>36.4</td><td>31.2</td><td>25.1</td><td>20.9</td><td>18.5</td><td>14.2</td><td>11.7</td><td class="box">10.1</td><td>8.6</td><td>7.0</td><td>6.0</td><td>5.2</td><td>4.6</td><td>4.2</td><td>3.8</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>t</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;9</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em> ≤ geometic mean citations is 9.</p>

    <div id="mu_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>μ-</em>index</h2>
      <p>If the <em>h-</em> and <em>g-</em>indices are the largest value <em>x</em> for which <em>x</em> publications have a minimum (<em>h</em>) or mean (<em>g</em>) number of citations equal to <em>x,</em> then the <em>μ-</em>index (Glänzel and Schubert 2010) is the same idea, except based on the median number of citations for the top <em>μ</em> publications.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td class="box">10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Median Citations</th><td>49.0</td><td>38.0</td><td>27.0</td><td>25.0</td><td>23.0</td><td>18.0</td><td>13.0</td><td>11.5</td><td>10.0</td><td class="box">10.0</td><td>10.0</td><td>6.5</td><td>3.0</td><td>3.0</td><td>3.0</td><td>3.0</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>μ</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;10</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em> ≤ median citations is 10.</p>

    <div id="Woeginger_w_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>w-</em>index (Woeginger)</h2>
      <p>Woeginger's <em>w-</em>index (Woeginger 2008) is somewhat similar to the <em>h-</em>index. It is the largest value of <em>w</em> for which publications have at least 1, 2, 3, …<em>w</em> citations:</p>$$w=\underset{k}{\max}\left(C_i \geq k-i+1\right),$$<p>for all <em>i</em> ≤ <em>k</em>. Put another way, the top 1…<em>k</em> publications have to have at least <em>k</em>…1 citations, respectively.</p><p>Graphically, if the <em>h-</em>index is the largest square with sides <em>h</em> that can fit under the citation curve, <em>w</em> is the largest right-angled isoceles triangle with perpendicular sides of <em>w</em>.</p><div id="chart_woeginger_w_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><td></td><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><td></td><th>Rank (<em>k</em>)</th><td><em>k</em>…1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>1</th><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>2</th><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>3</th><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>4</th><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>5</th><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>6</th><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>7</th><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>8</th><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td><em>w</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;9</td><th>9</th><td class="box">9</td><td class="box">8</td><td class="box">7</td><td class="box">6</td><td class="box">5</td><td class="box">4</td><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">2</td><td class="box">1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>10</th><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>11</th><td>11</td><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>12</th><td>12</td><td>11</td><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>13</th><td>13</td><td>12</td><td>11</td><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>14</th><td>14</td><td>13</td><td>12</td><td>11</td><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>15</th><td>15</td><td>14</td><td>13</td><td>12</td><td>11</td><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td></td><th>16</th><td>16</td><td>15</td><td>14</td><td>13</td><td>12</td><td>11</td><td>10</td><td>9</td><td>8</td><td>7</td><td>6</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where the minimum number of citations for publications 1…<em>k</em> ≤ <em>k</em>…1 is 9.</p>

    <div id="h2_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>h</em>(2)-index</h2>
      <p>The <em>h</em>(2)-index (Kosmulski 2006) is similar to the <em>h</em>-index, but rather than define the core based on <em>h</em> publications having <em>h</em> citations, this index requires the <em>h</em> publications to each have <em>h</em><sup>2</sup> citations:</p>$$h\left(2\right)=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i^2 \leq C_i\right).$$<div id="chart_h2_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>This leads to <em>h</em>(2) having a stricter definition of the core publications than many other metrics.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td class="box">23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank-squared (<em>i</em><sup>2</sup>)<td>1</td><td>4</td><td class="box">9</td><td>16</td><td>25</td><td>36</td><td>49</td><td>64</td><td>81</td><td>100</td><td>121</td><td>144</td><td>169</td><td>196</td><td>225</td><td>256</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>(2)&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em><sup>2</sup> ≤ <em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 3.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

    <div id="Wu_w_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>w-</em>index (Wu)</h2>
      <p>Wu's <em>w-</em>index (Wu 2010) is very similar to the <em>h-</em>index, but defines a stricter core by requiring that each of the <em>w</em> publications have at least 10<em>w</em> citations.</p>$$w=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq 10C_i\right)$$<div id="chart_wu_w_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>One can view this graphically as identical to the <em>h-</em>index, except the threshold line has a slope of 10 rather than 1.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td class="box">27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>10&times;Rank (10<em>i</em>)<td>10</td><td class="box">20</td><td>30</td><td>40</td><td>50</td><td>60</td><td>70</td><td>80</td><td>90</td><td>100</td><td>110</td><td>120</td><td>130</td><td>140</td><td>150</td><td>160</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td><em>w</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where 10<em>i</em> ≤ <em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 2.</p>

    <div id="hg_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>hg-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>hg-</em>index (Alonso <em>et al.</em> 2010) is an aggregate index which tries to keep the advantages of both the <em>h-</em> and <em>g-</em>indices while minimizing their disadvantages. The index is simply the geometric mean of <em>h</em> and <em>g,</em> or</p>$$hg=\sqrt{h \times g}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="a_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>a-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>a-</em>index (Jin 2006; Rousseau 2006) is used to describe the citations within the core itself, being simply the average number of citations per core publication, or</p>$$a=\frac{C^H}{h}=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{C_i}}{h}.$$<p>The minimum value of <em>a</em> is <em>h</em> (since every one of the <em>h</em> publications must have at least <em>h</em> citations).</p>

@@
    <div id="R_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>R-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>R-</em>index (Jin <em>et al.</em> 2007) is a measure of the quality of the Hirsch core, designed to avoid punishing scientists with larger cores. As a simple arithmetic average, the <em>a-</em>index has the size of the core in the divisor and therefore can lead to smaller values for scientists with much larger cores than those with much smaller cores (this is not an issue of the indices are only being used to compare those with similar sized cores). The <em>R-</em>index uses the square-root of the citations in the core rather than average:</p>$$R=\sqrt{C^H}=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{C_i}}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>R</em></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">1.0000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">3.4641</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">6.4031</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">9.0000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">11.4891</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">14.5602</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">18.3576</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">23.2379</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">28.6007</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">34.5254</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2007</td><td class="cell_value">39.9750</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2008</td><td class="cell_value">45.4423</td></tr>

    <div id="rational_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>rational <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The rational <em>h-</em>index (Ruane and Tol 2008) (<em>h</em><sup>Δ</sup> or <em>h</em><sub>rat</sub>) is a continuous version of <em>h</em> which not only measures the standard <em>h-</em>index but includes the fractional progress toward the next higher value of <em>h.</em> It is calculated as</p>$$h^\Delta = h + 1 -\frac{n}{2h+1},$$where \(n\) is the number of citations necessary to reach the next value of \(h\). The divisor, \(2h+1\), is the maximum number of possible citations needed to move from \(h\) to \(h+1\) (one additional citation for each of the \(h\) publications in the core plus \(h+1\) citations for a publication outside of the core with no citations). Practically speaking, \(n\) is the number of publications in the core with exactly \(h\) citations (thus needing one more to allow a move to \(h+1\)) plus \(h+1-C_{h+1}\) (the number of citations the \(h+1\)<sup>th</sup> ranked publication needs to reach \(h+1\) citations).</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The <em>h-</em>index is 6. To reach an <em>h</em> of 7, we would need to add 0 citations to the publications within the core and 4 citations to the 7<sup>th</sup> publication (for a total of 4). The maximum number of citations that could be required is 13 (if all 6 core publications had exactly 6 citations and the 7<sup>th</sup> publication had zero), thus the rational <em>h</em> is 6 + 1 - 4/13 = 6.6923.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="real_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>real <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>One can calculate the real <em>h-</em>index (Guns and Rousseau 2009), also known as the <span class="metric_name">interpolated <em>h-</em>index</span>, as the point at which the linear interpolation between the citation counts associated with publications <em>h</em> and <em>h</em> + 1 crosses a line with slope one,</p>$$h_r=\frac{\left(h+1\right)C_h-hC_{h+1}}{1-C_{h+1}+C_h}.$$<div id="chart_real_h_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div>The real <em>h-</em>index has the same graphical definition as <em>h,</em> except it is not restricted to integer values and thus represents the actual point where the citation and threshold curves cross.</p>

    <div id="tapered_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>tapered <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>While the rational <em>h-</em>index gives a fractional value to those citations necessary to reach the next value of <em>h,</em> the tapered <em>h-</em>index (Anderson <em>et al.</em> 2008) is designed to give every citation for every publication some fractional value. The best way to understand this index is to first consider the contribution of every citation to the <em>h-</em>index. To have an <em>h-</em>index of 1, an author needs a single paper with a single citation. That citation has a weight (or score) of 1, because it accounts for the entire <em>h</em> value of 1. To move to an <em>h-</em>index of 2, the author needs three additional citations: one additional citation for the original publication and two citations for a second publication. As <em>h</em> has increased by one, each of these three citations is contributing a weight (or score) of 1/3 to the total <em>h-</em>index. This is most easily illustrated by a Ferrers graph of ranked publications versus citations which shows the specific contribution of every citation to a specific value of <em>h</em></p><table class="ferrers"><tr><th></th><th></th><th colspan=7>Citation</th></tr><tr><td></td><td></td><td class="tc_bb">1</td><td class="tc_bb">2</td>  <td class="tc_bb">3</td><td class="tc_bb">4</td>  <td class="tc_bb">5</td><td class="tc_bb">6</td><td class="tc_bb">→</td></tr><tr><th rowspan=6>Ranked<br/>publication</th>  <td class="tc_rb">1</td><td class="tc_bdb tc_rdb">1</td><td class="tc_rdb">1/3</td>  <td class="tc_rdb">1/5</td><td>1/7</td><td>1/9</td><td>1/11</td></tr><tr><td class="tc_rb">2</td><td class="tc_bdb">1/3</td><td class="tc_bdb tc_rdb">1/3</td>  <td class="tc_rdb">1/5</td><td>1/7</td></tr><tr><td class="tc_rb">3</td><td class="tc_bdb">1/5</td><td class="tc_bdb">1/5</td>  <td class="tc_bdb tc_rdb">1/5</td><td>1/7</td></tr><tr><td class="tc_rb">4</td><td>1/7</td><td>1/7</td></tr><tr><td class="tc_rb">5</td><td>1/9</td></tr><tr><td class="tc_rb">↓</td></tr></table><p>The largest filled-in square in the upper left corner (the Durfee square) has a length equal to <em>h;</em> the contents of the square also sum to <em>h.</em> Using this logic, one can determine the credit each citation would give to a larger value of <em>h,</em> regardless of whether that <em>h</em> has been reached. Consider this graph with respect to the rational <em>h-</em>index. In the above example, <em>h</em> is 3. If one just considers the citations necessary to reach an <em>h</em> of 4, we can see that 5 of the 7 necessary citations are already present. Each of these has a weight of 1/7 (since 7 total citations are necessary); adding these to <em>h</em> we get the rational <em>h-</em>index, \(h^\Delta=3.71\). The tapered <em>h-</em>index is simply taking this same concept but expanding it to include all citations for all publications.</p><p>The tapered <em>h-</em>index for a specific publication is the sum of all of its scores and the total score of the index is the sum across all publications. In simple formulaic terms, the score <em>h<sub>T</sub></em>(<em>i</em>) for the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> ranked publication is calculated as</p>$$h_{T\left(i\right)}=\left |\begin{matrix} \frac{C_i}{2i-1} & \text{if } C_i \leq i \\ \frac{i}{2i-1}+\sum\limits_{j=i+1}^{C_i}{\frac{1}{2j-1}} & \text{if } C_i > i \end{matrix}\right. ,$$<p>and the total tapered <em>h-</em>index is the sum of these scores for all publications,</p>$$h_T=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{h_{T\left(i\right)}}.$$This index is consistent with the concept of the <em>h-</em>index, while also giving every citation some small influence on the score.</p>

    <div id="Todeschini_j_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>j-</em>index (Todeschini)</h2>
      <p>The <em>j-</em>index (Todeschini 2011) is a modification of the <em>h-</em>index which allows for over-cited publications in the core to increase the overall value of the index. It uses a set of fixed categorical increases over <em>h:</em></p><table class="j_table"><tr class="top_row"><th><em>k</em></th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td></tr><tr><th>Δ<em>h<sub>k</sub></em></th><td>500</td><td>250</td><td>100</td><td>50</td><td>25</td><td>10</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1.5</td><td>1.25</td></tr></table>$$j=h+\frac{\sum\limits_{k=1}^{12}{w_kX_k\left(h\times \Delta h_k\right)}}{\sum\limits_{k=1}^{12}{w_k} } ,$$<p>where \(w_k\), the weight given to each category, is simply \(1/k\), and \(X_k\left(h \times \Delta h_k\right)\) is the count of publications whose citations are at least equal to \(h \times \Delta h_k\).</p><p>Essentially this metric adds additional scores to <em>h</em> for publications which are cited well more than that necessary for the core, with larger weight given to those much higher than the core value (500 times the core get a weight of 1, 250 times the core get a weight of 0.5, etc.).</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p></p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th><em>k</em></th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><tr><th>Δ<em>h<sub>k</sub></em></th><td>500</td><td>250</td><td>100</td><td>50</td><td>25</td><td>10</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1.5</td><td>1.25</td><tr><th><em>h</em>&times;Δ<em>h<sub>k</sub></em></th><td>3000</td><td>1500</td><td>600</td><td>300</td><td>150</td><td>60</td><td>30</td><td>24</td><td>18</td><td>12</td><td>9.0</td><td>7.5</td><tr><th>Count <em>C<sub>i</sub></em> ≥ <em>h</em>&times;Δ<em>h<sub>k</sub></em> (<em>X<sub>k</sub></em>)</th><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>6</td><td>6</td><tr><th>Weight (<em>w<sub>k</sub></em>)</th><td>1.00</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.14</td><td>0.12</td><td>0.11</td><td>0.10</td><td>0.09</td><td>0.08</td><tr><th><em>w<sub>k</sub></em><em>X<sub>k</sub></em></th><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.14</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.40</td><td>0.55</td><td>0.50</td></table><p>The sum of <em>w<sub>k</sub></em><em>X<sub>k</sub></em> = 2.1716 and the sum of <em>w<sub>k</sub></em> = 3.1032, therefore <em>j</em> = 6 + 2.1716/3.1032 = 6.6998.</p>

    <div id="Wohlin_w_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>w-</em>index (Wohlin)</h2>
      <p>Wohlin's <em>w-</em>index (Wohlin 2009) is similar to others that try to address the issue where not all citations are included in the <em>h-</em>index and that many different distributions of citations can have identical <em>h-</em>indices. Unlike the other indices, however, it does not start with <em>h,</em> and instead uses a somewhat complicated procedure of dividing papers into classes based on the number of citations. Rather than give publications more weight for every citation, this index gives more weight to citations as the publication moves from one class to the next. Publications with fewer than five citations are ignored (given a weight of 0). The first class represents publications with 5-9 citations; each subsequent class has a width double that of the previous class, thus the second class represents 10-19 citations, the third class 20-39 citations, etc. This structure was chosen (other classification schemes could be substituted) because citations curves are usually skewed with many publications with relatively smaller numbers of citations, and few publications with relative large numbers of citations.</p><table class="cds_table"><tr><th>Class<br/>(<em>c</em>)</th><th>Citation Range</th><th>ln (lower limit)<br/>(<em>T<sub>c</sub></em>)</th><th>Cumulative<br/>Sum of <em>T<sub>c</sub></em><br />(<em>V<sub>c</sub></em>)</th></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>0&ndash;4</td><td>0.0000</td><td>0.0000</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>5&ndash;9</td><td>1.6094</td><td>1.6094</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>10&ndash;19</td><td>2.3026</td><td>3.9120</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>20&ndash;39</td><td>2.9957</td><td>6.9078</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>40&ndash;79</td><td>3.6889</td><td>10.5966</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>80&ndash;159</td><td>4.3820</td><td>14.9787</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>160&ndash;319</td><td>5.0752</td><td>20.0538</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>320&ndash;639</td><td>5.7683</td><td>25.8222</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>640&ndash;1279</td><td>6.4615</td><td>32.2836</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>1280&ndash;2559</td><td>7.1546</td><td>39.4382</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>2560&ndash;5119</td><td>7.8478</td><td>47.2860</td></tr><tr><td>etc.</td></tr></table><p>To calculate the metric, for each of the <em>c'</em> classes, one can count the number of publications within the <em>c</em><sup>th</sup> class, <em>X<sub>c</sub>.</em> Skewed distributions are often normalized using a logarithmic transform. Therefore, one calculates the natural logarithm of the lower limit of each class as <em>T<sub>c</sub></em> = ln(<em>B<sub>c</sub></em>) where <em>B<sub>c</sub></em> is the lower limit of the <em>c</em><sup>th</sup> class. One can also calculate <em>V<sub>c</sub></em> as the cumulative sum of <em>T<sub>c</sub></em> for all classes from 1 to <em>c.</em> The <em>w-</em>index is then calculated as</p>$$w=\sum\limits_{c=1}^{c^\prime}{X_c V_c}$$<p>The <em>w-</em>index increases as a publication moves from one class to the next. Moving between larger classes gives more weight than moving between smaller classes. Because it considers citations more broadly, the <em>w-</em>index is more fine-grained than the <em>h-</em>index.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr></table><p></p><table class="cds_table"><tr><th>Class<br/>(<em>c</em>)</th><th>Citation<br/>Range</th><th>Count of Pubs<br/>in Range<br/>(<em>X<sub>c</sub></em>)</th><th>ln (lower limit)<br/>(<em>T<sub>c</sub></em>)</th><th>Cumulative<br/>Sum of <em>T<sub>c</sub></em><br />(<em>V<sub>c</sub></em>)</th><th><em>X<sub>c</sub></em><em>V<sub>c</sub></em></th></tr><tr><td>0</td><td>0&ndash;4</td><td>10</td><td>0.0000</td><td>0.0000</td><td>0.0000</td></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>5&ndash;9</td><td>0</td><td>1.6094</td><td>1.6094</td><td>0.0000</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>10&ndash;19</td><td>3</td><td>2.3026</td><td>3.9120</td><td>11.7361</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>20&ndash;39</td><td>2</td><td>2.9957</td><td>6.9078</td><td>13.8155</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>40&ndash;79</td><td>1</td><td>3.6889</td><td>10.5966</td><td>10.5966</td></tr></table><p>The index is the sum of <em>X<sub>c</sub></em><em>V<sub>c</sub></em>, thus <em>w</em> = 36.1482.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>w</em></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">0.0000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">1.6094</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">10.8198</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">17.2575</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">36.1482</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">53.1826</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">89.1077</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">120.2044</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">160.7584</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">208.2201</td></tr>

    <div id="hj_indices" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>H<sub>j</sub>-</em>indices</h2>
      <p>The <em>H<sub>j</sub></em>-indices (Dorta-González and Dorta-González 2010) are essentially a multivariate cross between <em>h</em><sup>Δ</sup> and <em>w</em>(<em>q</em>). Like <em>h</em><sup>Δ</sup>, they attempt to discriminate amongst researchers with identical <em>h</em> values by comparing the upper and lower parts of the core to measure how close an author is to moving from one <em>h</em> to the next larger <em>h</em>. These indices are repeated for a series of <em>j</em>'s, where each <em>j</em> indicates the next higher value of <em>h</em> or <em>h</em> + <em>j</em>. Like <em>w</em>(<em>q</em>), the measures are in raw numbers of publications (rather than scaled) so can be a bit more cumbersome to interpret; furthermore they don't measure the missing number of citations, but rather total numbers and can include citations above-and-beyond those necessary to reach a particular <em>j</em>.</p><p>The basic calculation starts with the number of papers in the central core, thus</p>$$H_0=h^2.$$<p>Each subsequent value is then calculated as</p>$$H_j=H_{j-1}+\left( C_{h-j} - C_{h-j+1} \right) \left(h-j\right)+C_{h+j}.$$For <em>H</em><sub>1</sub>, this is essentially the number of citations necessary to reach <em>h,</em> plus the citations currently in the next paper outside of the core, plus the minimum number of citations over <em>h</em> common to all publications within the core. It is this last part that can make interpretation so difficult since a well over-cited core can lead to very large increases in subsequent values of <em>H<sub>j</sub>.</em> </p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="normalized_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>normalized <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The normalized <em>h-</em>index (Sidiropoulos et al. 2007) is simply the <em>h-</em>index divided by the number of publications (or the <em>v-</em>index expressed as a proportion rather than a percentage):</p>$$h^n=\frac{h}{P}.$$

    <div id="v_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>v-</em>index</h2>

    <div id="weighted_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>weighted <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>Similar to the <em>R-</em>index, the weighted <em>h-</em>index (Egghe and Rousseau 2008) is designed to give more weight to publications within the core as they gain citations. The primary difference is that for this metric the core is defined differently. Publications are still ranked by citation count, but instead of using the raw rank, one uses a weighted rank of</p>$$r_w\left(i\right)=\frac{\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{C_j}}{h},$$<p>that is, the weighted rank of the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication is the cumulative sum of citations for the top <em>i</em> publications, divided by the standard <em>h-</em>index. With these weighted ranks, one finds the last publication in the weighted core, <em>r</em><sub>0</sub>, as the largest value of <em>i</em> where \(r_w\left(i\right)\leq C_i\) (the last publication for which the weighted rank of that publication is less than or equal to the number of citations for that publication):</p>$$r_0=\underset{i}{\max}\left(r_w\left(i\right)\leq C_i\right).$$<p>The weighted index is then calculated as$$h_w=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{r_0}{C_i}},$$the square-root of the sum of citations for the weighted core.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td class="box">23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="dark_box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="dark_box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em> = 6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Cumulative Citations (Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>76</td><td>99</td><td>112</td><td>122</td><td>132</td><td>135</td><td>138</td><td>141</td><td>143</td><td>144</td><td>145</td><td>146</td><td>146</td><td>146</td><td>146</td></tr><tr><th><em>r<sub>w</sub></em>(<em>i</em>) = Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> / <em>h</em></th><td>8.17</td><td>12.67</td><td class="box">16.50</td><td>18.67</td><td>20.33</td><td>22.00</td><td>22.50</td><td>23.00</td><td>23.50</td><td>23.83</td><td>24.00</td><td>24.17</td><td>24.33</td><td>24.33</td><td>24.33</td><td>24.33</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>r</em><sub>0</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>r<sub>w</sub></em>(<em>i</em>) ≤ <em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 3. The weighted <em>h-</em>index is the square-root of the sum of citations up to this rank, thus <em>h<sub>w</sub></em> = √99 = 9.9499</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>h<sub>w</sub></em></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">1.0000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">3.0000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">6.0828</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">7.1414</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">9.9499</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">12.0416</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">15.3623</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">21.0713</td></tr>

    <div id="pi_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>π-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>π-</em>index (Vinkler 2009) is similar to other measures of the quality of the Hirsch core, except that it uses its own unique definition of the core. For this index, the core publication set <em>P<sub>π</sub></em> is defined as the square-root of the total number of publications, truncated down to the nearest integer (<em>e.g.</em>, for 80 publications, the square-root of 80 is 8.944, so <em>P<sub>π</sub></em> would equal 8):</p>$$P_\pi=\text{floor}\left({\sqrt{P}}\right).$$<p>The <em>π-</em>index is 1/100<sup>th</sup> of the total citations within this core:</p>$$\pi=\frac{C^\pi}{100}=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P_\pi}{C_i}}{100}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">49</td><td class="box">27</td><td class="box">23</td><td class="box">13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>P<sub>π</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;4</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The <em>π-</em>core is the floor of the square-root of the total number of publications, thus <em>P<sub>π</sub></em> = floor(√16) = floor(4.00) = 4. The <em>π-</em>index is 1/100<sup>th</sup> of the sum of the citations within the <em>π-</em>core, thus <em>π</em> = 112/100 = 1.12.</p>

    <div id="pi_rate" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>π-</em>core citation rate</h2>
      <p>The π-core citation rate, or π-rate, is the ratio of the π-core citations divided by the number of publications in the π-core. It is simply the average number of citations per core publication.</p>$$\pi\text{-rate}=\frac{C^\pi}{P_\pi}=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P_\pi}C_i}{P_\pi}=\frac{100\pi_\text{index}}{\mathrm{floor}\left(\sqrt{P}\right)}$$
      <h3>History</h3>

      <h2><em>q</em><sup>2</sup>-index</h2>

    <div id="e_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>e-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>e-</em>index (Zhang 2009) is simply a measure of the excess citations in the Hirsch core beyond those necessary to produce the core itself. It is measured as:</p>$$e=\sqrt{C^H-h^2}=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{C_i}-h^2}.$$<p>Graphically, it is the square-root of the total citations within the upper part of the citation curve.</p><div id="chart_e_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="maxprod_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>maxprod-index</h2>
      <p>The maxprod index (Kosmulski 2007) is the maximum value for the product between the number of citations for a publication and its rank, or,</p>$$MP=\max\left(i \times C_i\right).$$
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th>MP</th></tr>

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">40</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">69</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">110</td></tr>

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">266</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">402</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">568</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2007</td><td class="cell_value">750</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2008</td><td class="cell_value">936</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2009</td><td class="cell_value">1068</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2010</td><td class="cell_value">1206</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2011</td><td class="cell_value">1408</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2012</td><td class="cell_value">1645</td></tr>

    <div id="h2_upper_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>h</em><sup>2</sup>-upper index</h2>
      <p>The <em>h-</em>index describes an <em>h</em>×<em>h</em> square under the citation curve containing <em>h</em><sup>2</sup> citations. Bornmann <em>et al.</em> (2010) suggest dividing the citation curve into three sections based on this square and describing each section as the percent of all citations found within the section.</p><div id="chart_h2_upper_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>The <em>h</em><sup>2</sup>-upper index is the percent of excess citations found within the <em>h-</em>core citations, <em>i.e.</em>, the citations found above the <em>h-</em>square. It is calculated as:$$h_\text{upper}^2=\frac{C^H - h^2}{C^P}\times 100=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{C_i} - h^2}{C^P}\times 100=\frac{e^2}{C^P}\times 100.$$

      <h2><em>h</em><sup>2</sup>-tail index</h2>
      <p>The <em>h-</em>index describes an <em>h</em>×<em>h</em> square under the citation curve containing <em>h</em><sup>2</sup> citations. Bornmann <em>et al.</em> (2010) suggest dividing the citation curve into three sections based on this square and describing each section as the percent of all citations found within the section.</p><div id="chart_h2_tail_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div>The <em>h</em><sup>2</sup>-tail index is the percent of citations found within the tail of the citation curve, <em>i.e.</em>, the citations of publications outside the <em>h-</em>core. It is calculated as:$$h_\text{tail}^2=\frac{C^P - C^H}{C^P}\times 100=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=h+1}^{P}{C_i}}{C^P}\times 100$$

    <div id="k_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>k-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>k-</em>index (Ye and Rousseau 2010) is a measure of the relative impact of citations within the Hirsch core to those in the tail. Specifically, it is the ratio of impact over the tail-core ratio and is calculated as</p>$$k=\frac{C^PC^h}{P\left(C^P-C^h\right)}.$$This metric is specifically meant to be used in a time-series analysis where <em>k</em> is calculated for multiple time points.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">

    <div id="p_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>p-</em>index (Prathap)</h2>
      <p>Also called the <span class="metric_name">mock <em>h<sub>m</sub>-</em>index</span> (Prathap 2010b), the <em>p-</em>index (Prathap 2010a) is derived from mathematical modeling of the relationship of increasing numbers of publications and citations. It is a function of the total number of citations and the average citations per paper,</p>$$p=\sqrt[3]{\frac{\left(C^P\right)^2}{P}}.$$

    <div id="multidim_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>multidimensional <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The multidimensional <em>h-</em>index (García-Pérez 2009) is a simple expansion of the original <em>h-</em>index used to separate individuals with identical <em>h-</em>indices. The concept is to calculate the <em>h-</em>index from all <em>P</em> publications (this would be the first <em>h-</em>index or <em>h</em><sub>1</sub>). One can then calculate a second <em>h</em>-index, <em>h</em><sub>2</sub>, from the <em>P</em> – <em>h</em><sub>1</sub> remaining publications. Graphically, this is finding the largest square which can fit in the tail to the right of the original square represented by <em>h</em><sub>1</sub>. A third, <em>h</em><sub>3</sub>, can be calculated from the <em>P</em> – <em>h</em><sub>1</sub> – <em>h</em><sub>2</sub> remaining publications, etc., continuing until one reaches publications with 0 citations.</p><div id="chart_multidim_h_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>It should be obvious that <em>h</em><sub>1</sub> ≥ <em>h</em><sub>2</sub> ≥ <em>h</em><sub>3</sub>…</p><p>Unlike most of the other indices, this index set is primarily focused on the tail of the distribution, ignoring the excess/upper part of the citation curve completely. Because it is simply recalculating <em>h</em> for a smaller data set, its interpretation is quite straightforward and certainly could serve as a solid method of distinguishing individuals with identical <em>h.</em></p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest. Publications determined to be part of a core are removed for subsequent calcluations.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td     <div id="two_sided_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>two-sided <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The two-sided <em>h-</em>index (García-Pérez 2012) is an extension of the multidimensional <em>h-</em>index, which recalcultes <em>h</em> not only for the tail of the distribution (as in the multidimensional version) but also for the excess citations from <em>h-</em>core publications beyond those necessary to reach <em>h,</em> thus providing a multidimensional measure of both the upper and tail parts of the citation distribution.</p><p>The tail half of the two-sided <em>h-</em>index is identical to the multidimensional <em>h-</em>index (although, notationally, the original <em>h</em> is now designated <em>h</em><sub>0</sub>). To calculate the upper part, after the initial <em>h</em> is determined, one calculates <em>h</em><sub>-1</sub> (the value immediately in front of <em>h</em>) by first subtracting <em>h</em> from the citation count for all of the publications in the core, then determining a new <em>h</em> from this reduced citation set.</p><div id="chart_two_sided_h_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>Graphically, this is finding the largest square which can fit on top of the original square representing <em>h.</em> This process is repeated for the remaining excess citations in the (new) core.</p><p>To ensure symmetry, we calculate <em>h</em>±<em>k</em> such that the number of steps above the core is identical to the number of steps in the tail.</p></p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest. Publications within the original core have <em>h</em> citations removed at each step; publications in the original tail are ranked independently at each subsequent step.</p><table     <div id="iter_weighted_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>iteratively weighted <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The interatively weighted <em>h-</em>index (Todeschini and Baccini 2016) is a method for producing a single global index from the vector produced by the multidimensional <em>h-</em>index (<strong><em>H</em></strong>). This index is calculated as:</p>$$iw\left(h\right)=\sum\limits_{j=1}^{n}{\frac{h_j}{j}}.$$<p>where <em>h<sub>j</sub></em> and <em>n</em> are the <em>j</em><sup>th</sup> value and total length of <strong><em>H</em></strong>, respectively.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="EM_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>EM-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>EM-</em>index (Bihaari and Tripathi 2017) combines elements of the multidimensional <em>h-</em>index, the two-sided <em>h-</em>index, the iteratively weighted <em>h-</em>index, and the <em>e-</em>index. The <em>EM-</em>index begins by creating a vector (<strong><em>E</em></strong>) which is equivalent to the upper/excess half of the two-sided <em>h-</em>index, namely a series of <em>h</em> values calculated from the citation curve of just the core publications, stopping when one reaches only a single remaining publication, no citations remain, or all remaining publications have only a single citation. From this vector, <em>EM</em> can be calculated as:</p>$$EM=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{n}{E_i}},$$<p>where <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> and <em>n</em> are the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> element and length of <strong><em>E</em></strong>, respectively.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest. After each step, <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> is substracted from the citations of the top <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> publications. All publications beyond the top <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> are ignored at subsequent steps.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td class="light_box"></td><td class="light_box"></td><td     <div id="EMp_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>EM</em>&prime;-index</h2>
      <p>The <em>EM</em>&prime;-index (Bihari and Tripathi 2017) is an extension of the <em>EM-</em>index which includes all publications, rather than just those from the core. Like the <em>EM-</em>index, we begin by creating a vector (<strong><em>E</em></strong>) where the first value is <em>E</em><sub>1</sub> = <em>h.</em> Subsequent values of the vector, <em>E</em><sub>i+1</sub>, are determined by subtracting <em>E</em><sub>i</sub> from the citation count for all publications in the core defined by <em>E</em><sub>i</sub>, and recalculating <em>h</em> from these new citation counts, reranking all publications by these new citation counts as necessary (<em>i.e.</em>, some of the publications previously in the tail of the citation distribution may advance beyond publications in the core as citations representing earlier calculations of <em>h</em> are &ldquo;used up&rdquo;). This process continues until one runs out of citations, all of the remaining publications have only a single remaining citation, or there is only a single publication left to be considered. From this vector, one calculates the index as:</p>$$EM^\prime=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{n}{E_i}},$$<p>where <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> and <em>n</em> are the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> element and length of <strong><em>E</em></strong>, respectively.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest. After each step, <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> is subtracted from the citations of the top <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> publications and all publications are re-ranked by this adjusted citation count for the next step.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>E</em><sub>1</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr     <div id="hi_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index (Batista <em>et al.</em> 2006) is a simple correction of the <em>h-</em>index for multi-authored publications. This index is simply the <em>h-</em>index divided by the average number of authors in the core publications, or</p>$$h_i=\frac{h}{\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{A_i}}{h}}=\frac{h^2}{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{A_i}}.$$If every publication in the core is solo-authored then <em>h<sub>i</sub></em> = <em>h.</em> This can be an extremely harsh correction. A single core publication with a large number of co-authors may skew the average and thus lower an author's impact factor tremendously. Use of the median rather than the mean might be a fairer approach.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">2</td><td class="box">4</td><td class="box">1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr></table><p>The <em>h-</em>index is 6 and the sum of the authors for publications in the core is 16, thus <em>h<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2.2500.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>h<sub>i</sub></em></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">0.2500</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">1.1250</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">1.1250</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">2.2500</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">2.2500</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">2.3704</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">3.1842</td></tr>

    <div id="pure_h_index_frac" class="metric_container">
      <h2>pure <em>h-</em>index (fractional credit)</h2>
      <p>The pure <em>h-</em>index (Wan <em>et al.</em> 2007) is similar to the <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index in that it attempts to adjust for multiple authors. The index allows for different methods of assigning authorship credit. If one wishes to assign all authors equal credit, or if one does not have information about authorship order, one can assign fractional credit per author, which essentially means this metric is simply the <em>h-</em>index divided by the square-root of the average number of authors in the core, thus differing from <em>h<sub>i</sub></em> only by the square-root in the denominator (which makes the fractional version of the pure <em>h-</em>index less harsh than <em>h<sub>i</sub></em> by not punishing co-authorship as severely).</p>$$h_{p.\text{frac}}=\frac{h}{\sqrt{\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{A_i}}{h}}}$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">3</td><td class="box">2</td><td class="box">4</td><td class="box">1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr></table><p>The <em>h-</em>index is 6 and the sum of the authors for publications in the core is 16, thus <em>h<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3.6742.</p>

    <div id="pure_h_index_prop" class="metric_container">
      <h2>pure <em>h-</em>index (proportional credit)</h2>
      <p>The pure <em>h-</em>index (Wan <em>et al.</em> 2007) is similar to the <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index in that it attempts to adjust for multiple authors. The index allows for different methods of assigning authorship credit. If one has information on author order and assumes the order directly correlates with effort, one can use a proportional (artihmetic) assignment of credit for each publication as:</p>$$E_i=\frac{2\left(A_i + 1 - a_i\right)}{A_i\left(A_i + 1\right)},$$<p>where <em>a<sub>i</sub></em> is the position of the target author within the full author list of publication <em>i</em> (<em>i.e.</em>, an integer from 1 to <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>). Each publication can then be weighted by the inverse of the author effort, <em>w<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>. Given the credited effort for each publication, the metric is calculated as:</p>$$h_{p.\text{prop}}=\frac{h}{\sqrt{\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{w_i}}{h}}}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Position (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.50</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.40</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Weight (<em>w<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">2.00</td><td class="box">6.00</td><td class="box">2.00</td><td class="box">3.00</td><td class="box">5.00</td><td class="box">1.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr></table><p>The <em>h-</em>index is 6 and the sum of the weights for the publications in the core is 19.0, thus <em>h</em><sub><em>p</em>.prop</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3.3717.</p>

    <div id="pure_h_index_geom" class="metric_container">
      <h2>pure <em>h-</em>index (geometric credit)</h2>
      <p>The pure <em>h-</em>index (Wan <em>et al.</em> 2007) is similar to the <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index in that it attempts to adjust for multiple authors. The index allows for different methods of assigning authorship credit. If one has information on author order and assumes the order directly correlates with effort, one can use a geometric assignment of credit for each publication as:</p>$$E_i=\frac{2^{A_i - a_i}}{2^{A_i} - 1},$$<p>where <em>a<sub>i</sub></em> is the position of the target author within the full author list of publication <em>i</em> (<em>i.e.</em>, an integer from 1 to <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>). Each publication can then be weighted by the inverse of the author effort, <em>w<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>. Given the credited effort for each publication, the metric is calculated as:</p>$$h_{p.\text{geom}}=\frac{h}{\sqrt{\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{w_i}}{h}}}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Position (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.57</td><td>0.14</td><td>0.57</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.13</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.13</td><td>0.53</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.13</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.13</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Weight (<em>w<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">1.75</td><td class="box">7.00</td><td class="box">1.75</td><td class="box">3.00</td><td class="box">7.50</td><td class="box">1.00</td><td>7.50</td><td>1.88</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>7.50</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>7.50</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr></table><p>The <em>h-</em>index is 6 and the sum of the weights for the publications in the core is 22.0, thus <em>h</em><sub><em>p</em>.geom</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3.1334.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
