    <div id="adapt_pure_h_index_frac" class="metric_container">
      <h2>adapted pure <em>h-</em>index (fractional credit)</h2>
      <p>The adapted pure <em>h-</em>index (Chai <em>et al.</em> 2008) is very similar to the pure <em>h-</em>index, except that it estimates its own core rather than relying on the standard Hirsch core. For a given publication, if one wishes to assign all authors equal credit, or if one does not have information about authorship order, one can calculate an effective citation count as the number of citations divided by the square-root of the number of authors,</p>$$C^{*}_i = \frac{C_i}{\sqrt{A_i}}.$$<p>Publications are ranked according to these new citation counts and the <em>h-</em>equivalent value, <em>h<sub>e</sub>,</em> is found as the largest rank for which the rank is less than the number of equivalent citations, or</p>$$h_e = \underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq C^{*}_i\right).$$The adapted pure <em>h-</em>index is calculated by interpolating between this value and the next largest, as</p>$$h_{ap.\text{frac}}= \frac{\left(h_e+1\right)C^{*}_{h_e}-h_e C^{*}_{h_e +1}}{C^{*}_{h_e}-C^{*}_{h_e+1}+1}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>10</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(C^*_i\))</th><td>28.29</td><td>15.59</td><td>13.28</td><td>10.00</td><td class="box">9.19</td><td>5.00</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.71</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td class="box">5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h<sub>e</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where \(i \leq C^*_i\) is 5. Interpolating between this and the next largest rank yields <em>h</em><sub><em>ap</em>.frac</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5.8074.</p>

    <div id="adapt_pure_h_index_prop" class="metric_container">
      <h2>adapted pure <em>h-</em>index (proportional credit)</h2>
      <p>The adapted pure <em>h-</em>index (Chai <em>et al.</em> 2008) is very similar to the pure <em>h-</em>index, except that it estimates its own core rather than relying on the standard Hirsch core. For a given publication, if one has information on author order and assumes the order directly correlates with effort, one can use a proportional (artihmetic) assignment of credit for each publication as:</p>$$E_i=\frac{2\left(A_i + 1 - a_i\right)}{A_i\left(A_i + 1\right)},$$<p>where <em>a<sub>i</sub></em> is the position of the target author within the full author list of publication <em>i</em> (<em>i.e.</em>, an integer from 1 to <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>). Each publication can then be weighted by the inverse of the author effort, <em>w<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>. The effective number of citations for each publication is then calculated as</p>$$C^{*}_i = \frac{C_i}{\sqrt{w_i}}.$$<p>Publications are ranked according to these new citation counts and the <em>h-</em>equivalent value, <em>h<sub>e</sub>,</em> is found as the largest rank for which the rank is less than the number of equivalent citations, or</p>$$h_e = \underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq C^{*}_i\right).$$The adapted pure <em>h-</em>index is calculated by interpolating between this value and the next largest, as</p>$$h_{ap.\text{prop}}= \frac{\left(h_e+1\right)C^{*}_{h_e}-h_e C^{*}_{h_e +1}}{C^{*}_{h_e}-C^{*}_{h_e+1}+1}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>23</td><td>27</td><td>10</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Position (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.50</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.17</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.40</td><td>0.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.67</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Weight (<em>w<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>6.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>3.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>5.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(C^*_i\))</th><td>34.65</td><td>16.26</td><td>11.02</td><td>10.00</td><td largest rank where \(i \leq C^*_i\) is 5. Interpolating between this and the next largest rank yields <em>h</em><sub><em>ap</em>.prop</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5.6212.</p>

    <div id="adapt_pure_h_index_geom" class="metric_container">
      <h2>adapted pure <em>h-</em>index (geometric credit)</h2>
      <p>The adapted pure <em>h-</em>index (Chai <em>et al.</em> 2008) is very similar to the pure <em>h-</em>index, except that it estimates its own core rather than relying on the standard Hirsch core. For a given publication, if one has information on author order and assumes the order directly correlates with effort, one can use a geometric assignment of credit for each publication as:</p>$$E_i=\frac{2^{A_i - a_i}}{2^{A_i} - 1},$$<p>where <em>a<sub>i</sub></em> is the position of the target author within the full author list of publication <em>i</em> (<em>i.e.</em>, an integer from 1 to <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>). Each publication can then be weighted by the inverse of the author effort, <em>w<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>. The effective number of citations for each publication is then calculated as</p>$$C^{*}_i = \frac{C_i}{\sqrt{w_i}}.$$<p>Publications are ranked according to these new citation counts and the <em>h-</em>equivalent value, <em>h<sub>e</sub>,</em> is found as the largest rank for which the rank is less than the number of equivalent citations, or</p>$$h_e = \underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq C^{*}_i\right).$$The adapted pure <em>h-</em>index is calculated by interpolating between this value and the next largest, as</p>$$h_{ap.\text{geom}}= \frac{\left(h_e+1\right)C^{*}_{h_e}-h_e C^{*}_{h_e +1}}{C^{*}_{h_e}-C^{*}_{h_e+1}+1}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations largest rank where \(i \leq C^*_i\) is 5. Interpolating between this and the next largest rank yields <em>h</em><sub><em>ap</em>.geom</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5.5162.</p>

    <div id="normal_hi_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index</h2>
      <p>Similar to the adapted pure <em>h-</em>index, the normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index (Wohlin 2009) is designed to adjust the <em>h-</em>index for multiple authors by adjusting the citation count by the number of authors. The primary difference is the new citation value is calculated by dividing by the number of authors (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> / <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>) rather than the square-root of the number of authors. Publications are again ranked by these new citation per author values and the normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index is calculated in the same manner as the <em>h-</em>index, that is an author has a normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index of <em>h</em><sub><em>i</em>-norm</sub> when <em>h</em><sub><em>i</em>-norm</sub> of their publications have at least <em>h</em><sub><em>i</em>-norm</sub> citations per author, or</p>$$h_{i\text{-norm}}=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq \frac{C_i}{A_i}\right).$$<p>This is identical to what Egghe (2008) called the <span class="metric_name">fractional citation <em>h-</em>index (<em>h<sub>f</sub></em>)</span> and was again re-invented by Abbas (2011) as the <span class="metric_name">equally-weighted <em>h-</em>index (<em>h<sub>e</sub></em>)</span>.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>10</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>16.33</td><td>10.00</td><td>9.00</td><td>7.67</td><td class="box">6.50</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td class="box">5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em><sub><em>i-</em>norm</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em> ≤ <em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em> is 5.</p>

    <div id="hm_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>h<sub>m</sub>-</em>index</h2>
      <p>While the normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index accounts for the number of authors of a publication by normalizing the citation count for that publication, <em>h<sub>m</sub></em> (Schreiber 2008), also known as the <span class="metric_name">fractional paper <em>h</em></span> and <span class="metric_name"><em>h<sub>F</sub>-</em>index</span> (Egghe 2008), accounts for author number by normalizing the ranks. For this index, one counts the number of citations and ranks the publications as for the <em>h-</em>index, but rather than counting the rank of the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication as <em>i</em>, it is instead calculated as the cumulative sum of 1/<em>A<sub>i</sub>.</em> In formal terms, when calculating the <em>h-</em>index the rank of the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication is</p>$$r_i=\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{1}=i.$$<p>For <em>h<sub>m</sub></em> we instead calculate the effective rank as</p>$$r_{\text{eff}}\left(i\right)=\sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{\frac{1}{A_i}}.$$<p><em>h<sub>m</sub></em> is determined as the largest value of <em>r</em><sub>eff</sub>(<em>i</em>) for which <em>r</em><sub>eff</sub>(<em>i</em>) ≤ <em>C<sub>i</sub></em> or</p>$$h_m=\underset{r_{\text{eff}}\left(i\right)}{\max}\left(r_{\text{eff}}\left(i\right) \leq C_i\right).$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td class="box">3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.33</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.33</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.25</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.25</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Adjusted Rank (<em>r<sub>eff</sub></em>(<em>i</em>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;Σ<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.33</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.75</td><td>2.75</td><td class="box">3.00</td><td>3.25</td><td>4.25</td><td>5.25</td><td>5.50</td><td>6.00</td><td>7.00</td><td>7.25</td><td>7.75</td><td>8.75</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h<sub>m</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3.00</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></table><p>The largest adjusted rank where <em>r<sub>eff</sub></em>(<em>i</em>) ≤ <em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 3.0000.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="position_weighted_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>position-weighted <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The position-weighted <em>h-</em>index (Abbas 2011) is similar to the adapted pure <em>h-</em>index with proportional weighting in that it uses an author's position to weight citation counts prior to ranking, but differs by dividing the raw citation count directly by the inverse of the effort rather than the square-root of the effort. The effort is calculated as</p>$$E_i=\frac{2\left(A_i+1-a_i\right)}{A_i\left(A_i+1\right)},$$<p>with weight <em>w<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1/<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>, and the adjusted citation count as</p>$$C^{*}_i = \frac{C_i}{w_i} = C_i E_i,$$<p>where <em>a<sub>i</sub></em> is the position of the target author within the full author list of publication <em>i</em> (<em>i.e.</em>, an integer from 1 to <em>A<sub>i</sub></em>). Publications are ranked by these adjusted citation counts and then the metric is calculated as:</p>$$h_p=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq C^{*}_i\right).$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>23</td><td>10</td><td>27</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Position (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.50</td><td>0.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.33</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.40</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.67</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Weight (<em>w<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>6.00</td><td>3.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>5.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(C^*_i\))</th><td>24.50</td><td>11.50</td><td>10.00</td><td class="box">4.50</td><td>4.33</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.67</td><td>0.60</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td class="box">4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h<sub>p</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;4</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where \(i \leq C^*_i\) is 4.</p>

    <div id="prop_weight_cite_agg" class="metric_container">
      <h2>weighted citation aggregate (proportional)</h2>
      <p>The proportional weighted citation aggregate (Abbas 2011) is a weighted count of an author's citations (an alternative to <em>C<sup>P</sup></em>), where the weighting is based on the total number of authors of each publication and their position within the author list (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>) under an assumption of proportionally decreasing credit with respect to their position.</p>$$\psi_p=\sum\limits_{i=i}^{P}{\frac{C_i}{\frac{A_i\left(A_i+1\right)}{2\left(A_i+1-a_i\right)}}}=\sum\limits_{i=i}^{P}{C_i\frac{2\left(A_i+1-a_i\right)}{A_i\left(A_i+1\right)}}$$

    <div id="frac_weight_cite_h_cut" class="metric_container">
      <h2>weighted citation H-cut (fractional)</h2>
      <p>The fractional weighted citation H-cut is the sum of weighted citations found within an author's core publications, with the core defined by the normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index and the citation weighting using the scheme from the fractional weighted citation aggregate:</p>$$\xi_e=\sum\limits_{i=i}^{h_{i\text{-norm}}}{\frac{C_i}{A_i}}.$$<p>Note that for this metric publication order is by the weighted citation counts rather than the raw citation counts.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>10</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td class="box">16.33</td><td class="box">10.00</td><td class="box">9.00</td><td class="box">7.67</td><td class="box">6.50</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em><sub><em>i-</em>norm</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The sum of the adjusted citations for the top <em>h</em><sub><em>i-</em>norm</sub> publications is 49.49999999999999.</p>

    <div id="prop_weight_cite_h_cut" class="metric_container">
      <h2>weighted citation H-cut (proportional)</h2>
      <p>The proportional weighted citation H-cut is the sum of weighted citations found within an author's core publications, with the core defined by the position-weighted <em>h-</em>index and the citation weighting using the scheme from the proportional weighted citation aggregate:</p>$$\xi_p=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h_p}{C^{*}_i}=\sum\limits_{i=i}^{h_p}{\frac{C_i}{\frac{A_i\left(A_i+1\right)}{2\left(A_i+1-a_i\right)}}}=\sum\limits_{i=i}^{h_p}{C_i\frac{2\left(A_i+1-a_i\right)}{A_i\left(A_i+1\right)}}.$$<p>Note that for this metric publication order is by the weighted citation counts rather than the raw citation counts.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>23</td><td>10</td><td>27</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Position (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.50</td><td>0.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.33</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.40</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.67</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.67</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Weight (<em>w<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>6.00</td><td>3.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>5.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(C^*_i\))</th><td class="box">24.50</td><td class="box">11.50</td><td class="box">10.00</td><td class="box">4.50</td><td>4.33</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.20</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.67</td><td>0.60</td><td>0.20</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h<sub>p</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;4</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The sum of the adjusted citations for the top <em>h<sub>p</sub></em> publications is 50.5.</p>

    <div id="gf_cite" class="metric_container">
      <h2>fractional citation <em>g-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The fractional citation <em>g-</em>index (Egghe 2008) is a variant of the <em>g-</em>index normalized by the number of authors and is the <em>g</em> equivalent of the normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index. It is calculated as:</p>$$g_f=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i^2 \leq \sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{\frac{C_j}{A_j}}\right).$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>10</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Adjusted Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>16.33</td><td>10.00</td><td>9.00</td><td>7.67</td><td>6.50</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.50</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.75</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.25</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Cumulative Adjusted Citations (Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>16.33</td><td>26.33</td><td>35.33</td><td>43.00</td><td>49.50</td><td>52.50</td><td class="box">55.00</td><td>57.00</td><td>58.00</td><td>58.75</td><td>59.50</td><td>60.00</td><td>60.25</td><td>60.25</td><td>60.25</td><td>60.25</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Rank Squared (<em>i</em><sup>2</sup>)</th><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>9</td><td>16</td><td>25</td><td>36</td><td class="box">49</td><td>64</td><td>81</td><td>100</td><td>121</td><td>144</td><td>169</td><td>196</td><td>225</td><td>256</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>g<sub>f</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;7</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em><sup>2</sup> ≤ Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em> is 7.</p>

    <div id="gf_paper" class="metric_container">
      <h2>fractional <em>g-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The fractional <em>g-</em>index (Egghe 2008) is a variant of the <em>g-</em>index in which ranks are normalized by the number of authors and is the <em>g</em> equivalent of the <em>h<sub>m</sub>-</em>index. Effective ranks for each publication are calculated identically as for <em>h<sub>m</sub></em> and <em>g<sub>F</sub></em> is calculated as:</p>$$g_F=\underset{r_{\text{eff}}\left(i\right)}{\max}\left(r_{\text{eff}}\left(i\right)^2 \leq \sum\limits_{j=1}^{i}{C_j}\right).$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th>Adjusted Rank Squared (<em>r<sub>eff</sub></em>(<em>i</em>)<sup>2</sup></th><td>0.11</td><td>0.44</td><td>1.00</td><td>2.25</td><td>3.06</td><td>7.56</td><td>9.00</td><td>10.56</td><td>18.06</td><td>27.56</td><td>30.25</td><td>36.00</td><td>49.00</td><td>52.56</td><td>60.06</td><td largest adjusted rank where <em>r<sub>eff</sub></em>(<em>i</em>)<sup>2</sup> ≤ Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 8.7500.</p>

    <div id="fractional_p_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>fractional <em>p-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The fractional <em>p-</em>index (Prathap 2011) is a variant of the <em>p-</em>index which attempts to account for multiple-authored publications by adjusting both citation and publication counts by author counts. It is calculated as:</p>$$C^{\prime}=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\frac{C_i}{A_i}}$$$$P^{\prime}=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\frac{1}{A_i}}$$$$p_f=\sqrt[3]{\frac{\left.C^{\prime}\right.^2}{P^{\prime}}}$$

    <div id="harmonic_p_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>harmonic <em>p-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The harmonic <em>p-</em>index (Prathap 2011) is a variant of the <em>p-</em>index which attempts to account for multiple-authored publications by adjusting both citation and publication counts by author counts, using a harmonic weighting credit scheme based on the author's position within the authorship list for each publication (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>). For each publication, the author receives weighted credit equal to:$$r_i=\frac{\frac{1}{a_i}}{\sum\limits_{j=1}^{A_i}{\frac{1}{j}}}$$The harmonic <em>p-</em>index is then calculated as:</p>$$C^{\prime}=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{C_i r_i}$$$$P^{\prime}=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{r_i}$$$$p_h=\sqrt[3]{\frac{\left.C^{\prime}\right.^2}{P^{\prime}}}$$
      <h3>History</h3>

@@
    <div id="profit_p_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>profit <em>p-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The profit indices (Aziz and Rozing 2013) attempt to measure the effect of collaboration on an author's impact. They use a harmonic weighting algorithm and information on author order (assuming that authors in the middle of an author list had the least impact) to estimate effort for each publication. The effort for the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication is</p>$$E_i=\frac{1+\left|A_i+1-2a_i\right|}{\frac{1}{2}A_i^2+A_i\left(1-D_i\right)},$$<p>where</p>$$D_i=\begin{matrix} 0 & \text{if }A_i\text{ is even} \\ \frac{1}{2A_i} & \text{if }A_i\text{ is odd} \end{matrix}.$$<p>The sum of <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> for all publications is the number of &ldquo;monograph equivalents&rdquo; (a monograph being defined as a single-authored publication). The profit (<em>p</em>)-index is the relative contribution of collaborators to an individual's total publication record, or</p>$$p=1-\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{E_i}}{P}.$$<p>This value ranges from 0 to 1, with 0 indicating no contribution of co-authors (all solo-authored papers) and 1 meaning complete contribution from co-authors (a value of exactly 1 is impossible).</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">0.5048</td></tr>

@@
    <div id="profit_adj_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>profit adjusted <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The profit indices (Aziz and Rozing 2013) attempt to measure the effect of collaboration on an author's impact. They use a harmonic weighting algorithm and information on author order (assuming that authors in the middle of an author list had the least impact) to estimate effort for each publication. The effort for the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication is</p>$$E_i=\frac{1+\left|A_i+1-2a_i\right|}{\frac{1}{2}A_i^2+A_i\left(1-D_i\right)},$$<p>where</p>$$D_i=\begin{matrix} 0 & \text{if }A_i\text{ is even} \\ \frac{1}{2A_i} & \text{if }A_i\text{ is odd} \end{matrix}.$$<p>The sum of <em>E<sub>i</sub></em> for all publications is the number of &ldquo;monograph equivalents&rdquo; (a monograph being defined as a single-authored publication). The profit adjusted <em>h-</em> index is calculated by weighting each publications citation count by this effort,</p>$$C^{*}_i=E_i C_i,$$<p>reranking the publications based these weighted counts, and calculating the index as</p>$$h_a=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq E_iC_i\right)=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq C^{*}_i\right).$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>10</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Authors (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Position (<em>a<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Author Effort (<em>E<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>0.43</td><td>0.43</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.43</td><td>0.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.33</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.50</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(C^*_i\))</th><td>21.00</td><td>11.57</td><td>10.00</td><td>9.86</td><td class="box">6.50</td><td>3.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>1.67</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.00</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.50</td><td>0.17</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td class="box">5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h<sub>a</sub></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where \(i \leq C^*_i\) is 5.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>h<sub>a</sub></em></th></tr>

      <h2>profit <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The profit <em>h-</em>index is the ratio between the profit adjusted <em>h-</em>index (<em>h<sub>a</sub></em>) and the regular <em>h-</em>index, subtracted from one, roughly indicating the relative contribution of collaborators to an individual's h-index.</p>$$p_h=1-\frac{h_a}{h}$$

    <div id="total_self_cites" class="metric_container">
      <h2>total self citations</h2>
      <p>The total number of self citations is simply the number of citations to all of an author's publications made by other publications from the same author. If <em>s<sub>i</sub></em> is the number of self citations an author has made to their <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication then</p>$$S = \sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{s_i}.$$

      <h2>total self-citation rate</h2>
      <p>The total self-citation rate is the ratio bewtween the number of citations to all of an author's publications made by other publications from the same author versus the total number of citations to all of the author's publications or</p>$$S_r=\frac{S}{C^P}=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{s_i}}{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{C_i}}.$$

    <div id="mean_self_cite_rate" class="metric_container">
      <h2>mean self-citation rate</h2>
      <p>The mean self-citation rate is the average rate of self-citation for an author across all of their publications. It differs from the total self-citation rate in that the total is based on the ratio of the sums while the average is based on the sums of the ratios.</p>$$\bar{S_r}=\frac{1}{P}\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\frac{s_i}{C_i}}$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="sharpened_h_index_self" class="metric_container">
      <h2>sharpened <em>h-</em>index (self)</h2>
      <p>A simple way of accounting for self-citations with respect to impact is to simply remove them from each publication's citation counts prior to calculating a metric. For the base calculation of <em>h</em> this is known as the sharpened <em>h-</em>index (Schreiber 2007).</p><p>One complication is that there may be multiple methods for defining self-citations. In this case we remove any citations by the target author to their own publications.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>h</em><sub>sharp.self</sub></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">0</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">2</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">3</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">5</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">6</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">7</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">10</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">11</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">14</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">15</td></tr>

    <div id="b_index_mean_self" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>b-</em>index (mean self-citation rate)</h2>
      <p>The <em>b-</em>index (Brown 2009) is designed to correct <em>h</em> for self-citations, without actually having to check the citation records for every publication. It assumes that an author's self-citation rate is fairly consistent across publications such that, on average, a fraction <em>k</em> of the citations are from other authors. Assuming that citations follow a Zipfian distribution and that empirically derived estimates of the shape of this distribution are reasonable, one finds the index</p>$$b=hk^{\frac{3}{4}},$$<p>where <em>b</em> is an estimate of the <em>h-</em>index corrected for self-citations.</p><p>There are multiple ways to estimate the non-self-citation rate (<em>k</em>). In this case, we calculate it directly as the mean of the proportion of self-citations to total-citations across all publication, subtracted from one, or</p>$$k=1-\bar{S_r}=1-\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\frac{s_i}{C_i}}}{P}$$<p>where <em>s<sub>i</sub></em> is the number of self-citations by the target author to the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication.</p>

      <h2><em>b-</em>index (10% rate)</h2>
      <p>The <em>b-</em>index (Brown 2009) is designed to correct <em>h</em> for self-citations, without actually having to check the citation records for every publication.     <div id="h_rate" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>h-</em>rate</h2>
      <p>Originaly defined by Hirsch (2005), this metric is also known as the <span class="metric_name"><em>m-</em>quotient,</span> <span class="metric_name"><em>m-</em>ratio index,</span> <span class="metric_name">age-normalized <em>h-</em>index,</span> and <span class="metric_name">Carbon <em>h-</em>factor.</span> It measures the rate at which the <em>h-</em>index has increased over the career of a researcher. It is calculated simply as:</p>
$$m=\frac{h}{Y-Y_{0}+1},$$
<p>where \(h\) is the <em>h-</em>index in year \(Y\) and \(Y_{0}\) is the year of the researcher's first publication (the denominator of this equation is the academic age of the researcher).</p><div id="chart_h_rate_desc_div" class="time_chart"></div>
<p>The above estimation is essentially just the slope of the line from the start of a researcher's career (0 publications, 0 citations) through the most recent estimate of their <em>h-</em>index.</p>

      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">1.5714</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">1.5000</td></tr>

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">1.7000</td></tr>

    <div id="ls_h_rate" class="metric_container">
      <h2>least squares <em>h-</em>rate</h2>
      <p>Hirsch's original estimate of the <em>h-</em>rate was based on a single data point, the most recently measured value of <em>h</em> and the time since first publication. Burrell (2007) suggested that a more accurate measure could be estimated through least-squares regression of a series of <em>h-</em>indices measured at different time points of an author's career, while forcing the intercept through zero at the start of their career (prior to first publication).</p><div id="chart_ls_h_rate_desc_div" class="time_chart"></div></p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>m<sub>ls</sub></em></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">1.0000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">1.4000</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">1.1429</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">1.3333</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">1.2727</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">1.2967</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">1.3929</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">1.4265</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">1.4947</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">1.5481</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2007</td><td class="cell_value">1.5909</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2008</td><td class="cell_value">1.6446</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2009</td><td class="cell_value">1.7021</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2010</td><td class="cell_value">1.7320</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2011</td><td class="cell_value">1.7685</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2012</td><td class="cell_value">1.7975</td></tr>

    <div id="ar_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>ar-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>ar-</em>index (Jin 2007; Jin <em>et al.</em> 2007) is an adaptation of the <em>r-</em>index which includes time. Rather than being the square-root of the total citations within the core, it is the square-root of the citations per year within the core,</p>$$ar=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{h}{\frac{C_i}{Y-Y_i+1}}},$$<p>where <em>Y</em> is the current year.</p>

    <div id="dynamic_h_type_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>dynamic <em>h-</em>type-index</h2>
      <p>The dynamic <em>h-</em>type index (Rousseau and Ye 2008) is a measure of both the size of the core as well as how the core is changing through time. The basic index is</p>$$h_{dyn}=R\times v_h,$$where <em>R</em> is the <em>R-</em>index and <em>v<sub>h</sub></em> is a measure of the rate of change of <em>h</em> through time. Rousseau and Ye (2008) suggest a number of ways to estimate <em>v<sub>h</sub></em>, including that it be determined over a fixed time interval (<em>e.g.</em>, 5 or 10 years) to make it more contemporary. They also suggest one use the rational <em>h</em> rather than <em>h</em> because its finer grained resolution will allow for better estimates of the rate of change of <em>h.</em></p><p>Here <em>v<sub>h</sub></em> for a given year is estimated as the slope of the regression of the rational <em>h-</em>index against year for all data points up through that year.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">15.9636</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">20.8461</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">29.7327</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">38.4248</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">50.1405</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">62.8770</td></tr>

@@
    <div id="hpd_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>hpd-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The hpd-index (Kosmulski 2009) is very similar to the <em>h-</em>index, except that it adjusts for the age of a publication. Rather than adjust per year, the metric is adjusted per decade. Thus if</p>$$cpd_i=\frac{10C_i}{Y-Y_i+1}$$<p>is the number of citations an article has per decade (where <em>Y</em> is the current year), then the <em>hpd-</em>index for an author is the largest rank for which <em>hpd</em> of their publications (ranked by <em>cpd<sub>i</sub></em> rather than <em>C<sub>i</sub></em>) have <em>cpd</em> ≥ <em>hpd.</em></p>$$hpd=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq cpd_i\right)$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>23</td><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Age (<em>Y</em>&nbsp;&minus;&nbsp;<em>Y<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;+&nbsp;1)</th><td>2</td><td>5</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>5</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (<em>cpd<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>115.00</td><td>98.00</td><td>54.00</td><td>32.50</td><td>20.00</td><td>20.00</td><td>20.00</td><td>15.00</td><td>10.00</td><td class="box">10.00</td><td>6.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>5.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td class="box">10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>hpd</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;10</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em> ≤ <em>cpd<sub>i</sub></em> is 10.</p>

    <div id="contemporary_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>contemporary <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The contemporary <em>h-</em>index (Sidiropoulos <em>et al.</em> 2007) is designed to give more weight to the citations of recent publications and less weight to the citations of older publications. In its most general form, the contemporary score for a specific publication is</p>$$S^C_i=\gamma \left(Y-Y_i+1\right)^{-\delta}C_i.$$<p>The contemporary <em>h-</em>index for an author, <em>h<sup>C</sup></em>, is calculated similarly to the standard <em>h-</em>index, in that an author has a score of <em>h<sup>C</sup></em> if <em>h<sup>C</sup></em> of their articles (ranked by <em>S<sup>C</sup></em>) have <em>S<sup>C</sup></em> ≥ <em>h<sup>C</sup></em>.</p>$$h^C=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq S^C_i\right)$$Sidiropuolos <em>et al.</em> (2007) set <em>γ</em> = 4 and <em>δ</em> = 1. These choices have the consequence of making this metric virtually identical to <em>hpd,</em> except measured on a four year cycle rather than a decade.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>23</td><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Age (<em>Y</em>&nbsp;&minus;&nbsp;<em>Y<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;+&nbsp;1)</th><td>2</td><td>5</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>5</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(S^{C}_i\))</th><td>46.00</td><td>39.20</td><td>21.60</td><td>13.00</td><td>8.00</td><td>8.00</td><td class="box">8.00</td><td>6.00</td><td>4.00</td><td>4.00</td><td>2.40</td><td>2.00</td><td>2.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td class="box">7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h<sup>C</sup></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;7</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where \(i \leq S^{C}_i\) is 7.</p>

    <div id="trend_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>trend <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The trend <em>h-</em>index (Sidiropoulos <em>et al.</em> 2007) is essentially the opposite of the contemporary <em>h-</em>index. It is designed to measure how current an author's impact is by how recently they are being cited. The trend score for a publication is measured as</p>$$S^t_i = \gamma \sum\limits_{j=1}^{C_i}{\left(Y-Y_{j.i}+1\right)^{-\delta}}$$<p>where <em>γ</em> and <em>δ</em> are parameters (often set to 4 and 1, respectively, just as with the contemporary <em>h-</em>index) and <em>Y<sub>j.i</sub></em> is the year of the <em>j</em><sup>th</sup> citation for publication <em>i.</em> If the number of citations for publication <em>i</em> in year <em>Y<sub>k</sub></em> is <em>C<sub>i.k</sub></em>, this can also be written as $$S^t_i = \gamma \sum\limits_{k=1}^{Y}{C_{i.k}\left(Y-Y_k+1\right)^{-\delta}}.$$The trend <em>h-</em>index is the largest value for which an author has <em>h<sup>t</sup></em> publications with at least <em>S<sup>t</sup></em> ≥ <em>h<sup>t</sup></em>.</p>$$h^t = \underset{i}{\max}\left(i \leq S^t_i\right)$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by adjusted number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>23</td><td>27</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>3</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr ><th>&mdash;1997&mdash;</th><td>1</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>1</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td></tr><tr ><th>&mdash;1998&mdash;</th><td>5</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>1</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td></tr><tr ><th>&mdash;1999&mdash;</th><td>16</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>12</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td></tr><tr ><th>&mdash;2000&mdash;</th><td>11</td><td>8</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>6</td><td>0</td><td>2</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>&mdash;</td><td>&mdash;</td></tr><tr  class="top_row"><th>&mdash;2001&mdash;</th><td>16</td><td>15</td><td>9</td><td>5</td><td>4</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Adjusted Citations (\(S^{t}_i\))</th><td>113.13</td><td>76.00</td><td>61.00</td><td>32.33</td><td>26.33</td><td>22.67</td><td>12.00</td><td class="box">8.00</td><td>8.00</td><td>4.00</td><td>4.00</td><td>4.00</td><td>3.80</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td><td>0.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank largest rank where \(i \leq S^{t}_i\) is 8.</p>

    <div id="impact_vitality" class="metric_container">
      <h2>impact vitality</h2>
      <p>Impact Vitality (Rons and Amez 2008, 2009) is similar in concept to the trend <em>h-</em>index, but more complicated to measure. If <em>c<sup>x</sup></em> is the total number of citations (across all publications) from year <em>x,</em> and <em>w</em> is the number of years back from the present (year <em>Y</em>) one wishes to calculate the metric for (the citation window), then</p>$$IV\left(w\right)=\frac{w\left(\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{w}{\frac{c^{Y-w}}{i}}}{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{w}{c^{Y-w}}} \right)-1 }{\left(\sum\limits_{i=1}^{w}{\frac{1}{i} } \right)-1}.$$<p>The numerator of the numerator is the sum of citation counts divided by their age for the window of time in question; the denominator of the numerator is the total number of citations for the same window of time. An impact vitality score of 1 indicates that the number of citations is approximately constant over time. A value above 1 indicates that the number of citations is increasing through time, while a value below 1 indicates the number of citations is decreasing through time. Individuals with very different total numbers of citations can have identical scores because the metric is focused on proportional change and not absolute numbers. However, even beyond the issues of more difficult data collection, this metric has odd properties because of its overwhelming focus on immediacy. It would produce a higher score for someone with just 1 citation a year ago and no citations 2 years ago than another person with 1,000 citations 2 years ago and no citations one year ago.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">

    <div id="specific_impact_s_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>specific impact <em>s-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The specific-impact <em>s-</em>index (De Visscher 2010) is designed to avoid the age-bias of other indices as well as not penalizing fields where citations may lag due to the speed of the publication process. It is designed to predict the total number of citations a set of publications will have at a time infinitely in the future, assuming exponential aging of the citation process. The <em>s-</em>index is a measure of the projected citation rate per publication (rather than the actual citation rate per publication). The practical definition is</p>$$S=\frac{C^P}{10\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\left(1-e^{0.1\left(Y-Y_i\right)}\right)}}=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{C_i}}{10\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\left(1-e^{0.1\left(Y-Y_i\right)}\right)}},$$<p>where <em>s</em> is a measure of the citation rate per publication (divided by 10) projected to time infinity. The actual prediction of the total number of citations an author would have at time infinity would therefore be 10<em>sP</em>.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">

    <div id="Franceschini_f_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>f-</em>index (Franceschini &amp; Maisano)</h2>
      <p>Franceschini and Maisano's <em>f-</em>index (Franceschini and Maisano 2010) is designed as a complement to the <em>h-</em>index. It is a measure of the time-width of impact. It is the range of time for publications with at least one citation and is calculated as</p>$$f=\max\left(Y_i|C_i>0\right) - \min\left(Y_i|C_i>0\right) + 1,$$<p>or the year of the most recent publication with at least one citation minus the year of the earliest publication with at least one citation (plus one to consider the time spent preparing the earliest publication). For most active researchers, this will likely be simply the number of years since they first published since it will most often be the difference in age between their first publication (which probably has at least one citation) and their most recent publication to get a single citation (probably published within the last year or two).</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">

    <div id="annual_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>annual <em>h</em>-index</h2>
      <p>The annual <em>h</em>-index attempts to normalize by both the number of coauthors of each publication as well as the academic age of the researcher and is calculated as the normalized <em>h<sub>i</sub>-</em>index divided by academic age, or:</p>$$\text{hIa} = \frac{h_i}{Y-Y_0+1}.$$

@@
    <div id="CDS_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>citation distribution score index</h2>
      <p>The citation distribution score index (Vinkler 2011, 2013) is a weighted sum of publication counts in a set of pre-defined citation-count-based categories. The 14 categories are defined predominantly by their upper limits, with the <em>k</em><sup>th</sup> category usually having an upper limits of 2<sup><em>k</em></sup> citations, except for the first category which has an upper limit of 2<sup>0</sup> = 1 citation, and the last category, which has no upper limit.</p><table class="cds_table"><tr><th>Category</th><th>Range of<br/>Citations</th><th>Minimum<br/>Citations</th><th>Maximum<br/>Citations</th></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>0&ndash;2<sup>0</sup></td><td>0</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>(2<sup>0</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>2</sup></td><td>2</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>(2<sup>2</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>3</sup></td><td>5</td><td>8</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>(2<sup>3</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>4</sup></td><td>9</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>(2<sup>4</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>5</sup></td><td>17</td><td>32</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>(2<sup>5</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>6</sup></td><td>33</td><td>64</td></tr><tr><td>7</td><td>(2<sup>6</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>7</sup></td><td>65</td><td>128</td></tr><tr><td>8</td><td>(2<sup>7</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>8</sup></td><td>129</td><td>256</td></tr><tr><td>9</td><td>(2<sup>8</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>9</sup></td><td>257</td><td>512</td></tr><tr><td>10</td><td>(2<sup>9</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>10</sup></td><td>513</td><td>1024</td></tr><tr><td>11</td><td>(2<sup>10</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>11</sup></td><td>1025</td><td>2048</td></tr><tr><td>12</td><td>(2<sup>11</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>12</sup></td><td>2049</td><td>4096</td></tr><tr><td>13</td><td>(2<sup>12</sup>+1)&ndash;2<sup>13</sup></td><td>4097</td><td>8192</td></tr><tr><td>14</td><td>(2<sup>12</sup>+1)&ndash;&#x221e;</td><td>>8192</td><td>&#x221e;</td></tr></table><p>The rank of each category is also its weight. If <em>P<sup>k</sup></em> is the count of publications within the <em>k</em><sup>th</sup> category, then the index is calculated as</p>$$\text{CDS}=\sum\limits_{k=1}^{14}{k P^k}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr></table><p></p><table class="cds_table"><tr><th>Category<br/>(<em>k</em>)</th><th>Citation<br/>Range</th><th>Count of Pubs<br/>in Range<br/>(<em>P<sup>k</sup></em>)</th><th><em>kP<sup>k</sub></em></th></tr><tr><td>1</td><td>0&ndash;1</td><td>6</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><td>2</td><td>2&ndash;4</td><td>4</td><td>8</td></tr><tr><td>3</td><td>5&ndash;8</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><td>4</td><td>9&ndash;16</td><td>3</td><td>12</td></tr><tr><td>5</td><td>17&ndash;32</td><td>2</td><td>10</td></tr><tr><td>6</td><td>33&ndash;64</td><td>1</td><td>6</td></tr></table><p>The index is the sum of <em>kP<sup>k</sub></em>, thus CDS&nbsp;=&nbsp;42.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th>CDS</th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">5</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">11</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">18</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">31</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">42</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">62</td></tr>

    <div id="CDR_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>citation distribution rate index</h2>
      <p>The citation distribution rate index (Vinkler 2011, 2013) is a complement to the citation distribution score index, calculated as the percent of the maximum possible CDS index actually observed, or</p>$$\text{CDR}=100\frac{\text{CDS}}{\text{CDS}_\max}=100\frac{\text{CDS}}{14P}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="circ_cite_area_radius" class="metric_container">
      <h2>circular citation area radius</h2>
      <p>The circular citation area radius (Sangwal 2012 )is an easy to calculate approximation of the <em>h-</em>index.</p>$$r=\sqrt{\frac{C^P}{\pi}}=\sqrt{\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{C_i}}{\pi}}$$

    <div id="citation_acceleration" class="metric_container">
      <h2>citation acceleration</h2>
      <p>Citation acceleration (Sangwal 2012) is defined as the total number of citations of an author divided by the square of their academic age,</p>$$a = \frac{C^P}{\left(Y-Y_0+1\right)^2}=\frac{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{C_i}}{\left(Y-Y_0+1\right)^2}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="Redner_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>Redner index</h2>
      <p>The Redner index (Redner 2010) is just the square-root of the total citation count for all publications, similar to the Levene <em>j-</em>index, which is the sum of the square-root of the counts.</p>$$R=\sqrt{C^P}=\sqrt{\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{C_i}}$$

      <p>The Levene <em>j-</em>index (Levene <em>et al.</em> 2012) is the sum of the square-root of citation counts for all publications, similar to the Redner index which is the square-root of the sum.</p>$$j=\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\sqrt{C_i}}$$
      <h3>History</h3>

      <h2><em>S-</em>index (<em>h-</em>mixed synthetic index)</h2>
      <p>The <em>S-</em>index (Ye 2010) is an <em>h-</em>mixed synthetic index which combines multiple metrics together into a single value. It is calculated as </p>$$S=100\log_{10}\left(h \times C/P\right),$$<p>where C/P is the mean number of citations per publication.</p>

    <div id="T_index_h_mixed" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>T-</em>index (<em>h-</em>mixed synthetic index)</h2>
      <p>The <em>T-</em>index (Ye 2010) is an <em>h-</em>mixed synthetic index which combines multiple metrics together into a single value.     <div id="citation_entropy" class="metric_container">
      <h2>citation entropy (<em>s-</em>index)</h2>
      <p>Citation entropy attempts to characterize the quality and impact of scientific researchusing an entropy model. It is calculated as</p>$$s=\frac{1}{4}\sqrt{C^P}e^{H^*},$$<p>where <em>H</em><sup>*</sup> is the standardized Shannon entropy or</p>$$H^*=-\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{\left(\frac{C_i}{C^P}\log_2\frac{C_i}{C^P}\right)}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

    <div id="cq_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>corrected quality ratio</h2>
      <p>The corrected quality ratio (Lindsey 1978) is a metric derived from the total citations and the total publications. It is calculated as:</p>$$CQ=\frac{C^P}{P}\sqrt{C^P \times P}$$which is the product of the mean number of citations per publication and the geometric mean of the numbers of citations and publications. It was designed to overcome problems with the <em>C/P</em> index, particularly with respect to younger researchers.
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th><em>CQ</em></th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">1.2649</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">23.7171</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">121.7861</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">230.2274</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">441.0312</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">873.2981</td></tr>

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">4860.7421</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">8301.0238</td></tr>

    <div id="cq0_4_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>CQ<sup>0.4</sup> index</h2>
      <p>The <em>CQ</em><sup>0.4</sup> index (Glänzel 2008) is a modification of the <em>CQ</em> index, meant to better match the dimensionality of the <em>h-</em>index, calculated as:</p>$$CQ^{0.4}=\left(\frac{C^P}{P}\right)^{0.6}P^{0.4}$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="indifference" class="metric_container">
      <h2>indifference</h2>
      <p>This metric is simply the total number of publications divided by the number of total number of citations (Egghe and Rousseau 1996); it was originally proposed to evaluate journals but can be applied to individuals as well. The larger this value, the more indifferent the community is to the author's work. This metric is also just the inverse of the mean number of citations per publication (<em>C/P</em>).</p>$$D=\frac{P}{C^P}$$
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">

    <div id="th_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>characteristic time scale of scientific activity</h2>
      <p>The characteristic time scale of scientific activity of a researcher (<em>t<sub>h</sub></em> index) (Popov 2005) is the amount of time from the present to the past accounting for publications to which the researcher receives half of their citations. Put another way, if one sums a researcher's citations in reverse chronological order by publication, it is the number of years necessary to reach half of their total citations.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by year of publication, from most recent to oldest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Year (<em>Y<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2001</td><td>2001</td><td>2001</td><td>2001</td><td>2000</td><td>2000</td><td>2000</td><td>2000</td><td>2000</td><td>1999</td><td>1998</td><td class="box">1997</td><td>1997</td><td>1997</td><td>1997</td><td>1997</td></tr><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>23</td><td>3</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>3</td><td>13</td><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td></tr><tr><th>Cumulative Citations (Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>26</td><td>29</td><td>30</td><td>31</td><td>31</td><td>34</td><td>47</td><td class="box">96</td><td>123</td><td>133</td><td>143</td><td>146</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>½<em>C<sup>P</sup></em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;73.0</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></table><p>The first year where Σ<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> ≥ ½<em>C<sup>P</sup></em> is 1997, thus the characteristic time scale of scientific activity is 2001&nbsp;&minus;&nbsp;1997&nbsp;+&nbsp;1&nbsp;=&nbsp;5</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

    <div id="dci_index_2" class="metric_container">
      <h2>discounted cumulated impact (sharp decay)</h2>
      <p>The discounted cumulated impact index (Järvelin and Pearson 2008; Ahlfren and Järvelin 2010) is designed to reduce the role of older citations, but still reward researchers for older publications receiving new citations. To begin, let <em>c<sup>x</sup></em> represet the total number of citations received by a researcher to all of their publications in year <em>x</em>. Over a citation interval of <em>Y</em> years, the cumulated impact vector is simply the sum of these values from the first year to be considered (<em>Y</em> years ago) up through the current year. If the citation interval is the entire career of a researcher, this would be a vector of <em>C<sup>P</sup></em> calculated each year. This raw vector incorporates citation counts without decay over time, <em>i.e.</em>, old and new citations are treated equally. To incorporate a time decay, one divides the count of new citations each year by the logarithm of the number of years elapsed. The discounted cumulative impact vector is thus:</p>$$\text{DCI}_Y\left(i\right)=\left|\begin{matrix} \frac{c^i}{\max\left[1, \log_b\left(Y-1\right)\right]} & \text{if }i=1 \\ \text{DCI}_Y\left(i-1\right)+\frac{c^i}{\max\left[1, \log_b\left(Y-i\right)\right]} & \text{otherwise} \end{matrix} \right. ,$$<p>where <em>b</em> is the base of the logarithm used for scaling. Larger values of <em>b</em> discount older citations less than smaller values.</p><p>In this version we use a sharp decay with <em>b</em> = 2.</p>

    <div id="ddci_index_2" class="metric_container">
      <h2>dynamic discounted cumulated impact (sharp decay)</h2>
      <p>The dynamic discounted cumulated impact index (Järvelin and Pearson 2008; Ahlfren and Järvelin 2010) is an addendum to the discounted cumulated impact index which cumulates across different time intervals, rather than a single fixed interval. Formally, it is a vector defined as:</p>$$\text{dDCI}_Y\left[j\right]=DCI_j\left[j\right] ,$$<p>where DCI<em><sub>j</sub></em>[<em>j</em>] is essentially the last item in the DCI vector for time interval <em>j.</em> When calculated for a career, where each subsequent interval is the length of the career up to that point, the values calculated for earlier career points are identical those same intervals for later career points, thus we just report the final value for each year, with all values up to that year actually representing the full dDCI vector.</p><p>Because DCI can be calculated with varying degrees of citation decay, dDCI is also dependent on this same decay. This version is calculated from the DCI index with a sharp decay of <em>b</em> = 2.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="dci_index_10" class="metric_container">
      <h2>discounted cumulated impact (mild decay)</h2>
      <p>The discounted cumulated impact index (Järvelin and Pearson 2008; Ahlfren and Järvelin 2010) is designed to reduce the role of older citations, but still reward researchers for older publications receiving new citations. To begin, let <em>c<sup>x</sup></em> represet the total number of citations received by a researcher to all of their publications in year <em>x</em>. Over a citation interval of <em>Y</em> years, the cumulated impact vector is simply the sum of these values from the first year to be considered (<em>Y</em> years ago) up through the current year. If the citation interval is the entire career of a researcher, this would be a vector of <em>C<sup>P</sup></em> calculated each year. This raw vector incorporates citation counts without decay over time, <em>i.e.</em>, old and new citations are treated equally. To incorporate a time decay, one divides the count of new citations each year by the logarithm of the number of years elapsed. The discounted cumulative impact vector is thus:</p>$$\text{DCI}_Y\left(i\right)=\left|\begin{matrix} \frac{c^i}{\max\left[1, \log_b\left(Y-1\right)\right]} & \text{if }i=1 \\ \text{DCI}_Y\left(i-1\right)+\frac{c^i}{\max\left[1, \log_b\left(Y-i\right)\right]} & \text{otherwise} \end{matrix} \right. ,$$<p>where <em>b</em> is the base of the logarithm used for scaling. Larger values of <em>b</em> discount older citations less than smaller values.</p><p>In this version we use a mild decay with <em>b</em> = 10.</p>

    <div id="ddci_index_10" class="metric_container">
      <h2>dynamic discounted cumulated impact (mild decay)</h2>
      <p>The dynamic discounted cumulated impact index (Järvelin and Pearson 2008; Ahlfren and Järvelin 2010) is an addendum to the discounted cumulated impact index which cumulates across different time intervals, rather than a single fixed interval. Formally, it is a vector defined as:</p>$$\text{dDCI}_Y\left[j\right]=DCI_j\left[j\right] ,$$<p>where DCI<em><sub>j</sub></em>[<em>j</em>] is essentially the last item in the DCI vector for time interval <em>j.</em> When calculated for a career, where each subsequent interval is the length of the career up to that point, the values calculated for earlier career points are identical those same intervals for later career points, thus we just report the final value for each year, with all values up to that year actually representing the full dDCI vector.</p><p>Because DCI can be calculated with varying degrees of citation decay, dDCI is also dependent on this same decay. This version is calculated from the DCI index with a mild decay of <em>b</em> = 10.</p>

    <div id="history_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>history <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The history <em>h-</em>index (Randić 2009) is another metric designed to help distinguish among researchers with identical <em>h-</em>indices. It is based on the idea of exploring a power expansion of the citation counts for publications in the core. This index starts by creating a vector representing larger power expansions of citation counts. The <em>k</em><sup>th</sup> element of the vector is the largest rank (<em>i</em>), for which the number of citations for that publication is equal or larger than the rank times 2<sup><em>k</em></sup>, or</p>$$H^k=\underset{i}{\max}\left(C_i \geq i \times 2^k\right).$$<p>By definition, the first (zeroth) entry is <em>h</em> (since 2<sup>0</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1). Graphically, this is looking at the intersection (rounding the rank down to the nearest integer) of the citation curve with a series of lines with ever increasing slopes of 2<sup><em>k</em></sup>: 1 (=<em>h</em>), 2, 4, 8, etc., with the largest <em>k</em> equal to the line with the largest slope that actually intersects the citation curve.</p><div id="chart_history_h_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div><p>The history <em>h-</em>index is simply the sum of the values in this vector, or</p>$$H=\sum\limits_{k=0}^{K}{H^k},$$<p>where <em>K</em> is the largest power of 2 for which <em>C</em><sub>max</sub> >= 2<sup><em>K</em></sup>.
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th></th><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><th>2<sup>0</sup></th><th>2<sup>1</sup></th><th>2<sup>2</sup></th><th>2<sup>3</sup></th><th>2<sup>4</sup></th><th>2<sup>5</sup></th><th>2<sup>6</sup></th></tr><tr><td><em>H</em><sup>4</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1,&nbsp;<em>H</em><sup>5</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1</td><td>49</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>8</td><td class="box">16</td><td class="box">32</td><td>64</td></tr><tr><td><em>H</em><sup>3</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2</td><td>27</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>8</td><td class="box">16</td><td>32</td><td>64</td><td>128</td></tr><tr><td><em>H</em><sup>2</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td>23</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>6</td><td class="box">12</td><td>24</td><td>48</td><td>96</td><td>192</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>13</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>8</td><td>16</td><td>32</td><td>64</td><td>128</td><td>256</td></tr><tr><td><em>H</em><sup>1</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;5</td><td>10</td><td>5</td><td>5</td><td class="box">10</td><td>20</td><td>40</td><td>80</td><td>160</td><td>320</td></tr><tr><td><em>H</em><sup>0</sup>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td>10</td><td>6</td><td class="box">6</td><td>12</td><td>24</td><td>48</td><td>96</td><td>192</td><td>384</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>3</td><td>7</td><td>7</td><td>14</td><td>28</td><td>56</td><td>112</td><td>224</td><td>448</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>3</td><td>8</td><td>8</td><td>16</td><td>32</td><td>64</td><td>128</td><td>256</td><td>512</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>3</td><td>9</td><td>9</td><td>18</td><td>36</td><td>72</td><td>144</td><td>288</td><td>576</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>2</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>20</td><td>40</td><td>80</td><td>160</td><td>320</td><td>640</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>1</td><td>11</td><td>11</td><td>22</td><td>44</td><td>88</td><td>176</td><td>352</td><td>704</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>1</td><td>12</td><td>12</td><td>24</td><td>48</td><td>96</td><td>192</td><td>384</td><td>768</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>1</td><td>13</td><td>13</td><td>26</td><td>52</td><td>104</td><td>208</td><td>416</td><td>832</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>0</td><td>14</td><td>14</td><td>28</td><td>56</td><td>112</td><td>224</td><td>448</td><td>896</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>0</td><td>15</td><td>15</td><td>30</td><td>60</td><td>120</td><td>240</td><td>480</td><td>960</td></tr><tr><td></td><td>0</td><td>16</td><td>16</td><td>32</td><td>64</td><td>128</td><td>256</td><td>512</td><td>1024</td></tr></table><p>The sum of all of the <em>H<sup>k</sup></em> values is 18.</p>

    <div id="quality_quotient" class="metric_container">
      <h2>quality quotient</h2>
      <p>The quality quotient (Randić 2009) is the ratio between the history <em>h-</em>index and the <em>h-</em>index,</p>$$Q=\frac{H}{h}.$$<p>This metric can only really be used to compare individuals with identical <em>h-</em>indices, as the minimum possible value varies with <em>h</em> in a non-monotonic manner, <em>e.g.,</em> when <em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1, min(<em>Q</em>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;1; when <em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2, min(<em>Q</em>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.5; when <em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3, min(<em>Q</em>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.33; when <em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;4, min(<em>Q</em>)&nbsp;=&nbsp;1.75, etc.</p>

    <div id="scientist_level" class="metric_container">
      <h2>scientist's level</h2>
      <p>The scientist's level (Mitropoulos 2009) is an attempt to quantitatively break scientist's into broad categories of advancement based on a combination of their total citations and publications. It is expressed as <em>L<sup>v</sup>,</em> where <em>v</em> represent the overall level and <em>L</em> the progress within <em>v</em>. Functionally, these values are logarithmic decompositions of the sum of citations and publications, such that <em>v</em> represents the integer value of the (base 10) logarithm of the sum (<em>i.e.,</em> <em>v</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;0 when the sum is less than 10, <em>v</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1 when the sum is between 10 and 99, <em>v</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2 when the sum is between 100 and 999, etc.), and <em>L</em> is the integer value of the sum divided by 10<sup><em>v</em></sup> (<em>i.e.,</em> if <em>v</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2, <em>L</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;1 if the sum is between 100 and 199, <em>L</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;2 if the sum is between 200 and 299, etc.).</p> $$v=\text{floor}\left[\log\left(C^P + P\right)\right]$$$$L^v=\text{floor}\left(\frac{C^P + P}{10^v}\right)$$<p><em>L</em> can be any integer from 1 to 9, while <em>v</em> will generally be an integer between 0 and 5 (a <em>v</em> of 6 would require > 1 million combined citations and publications).</p><p>Because of this structure and the limited possible values of these measures, one could readily combine these into a single value as,</p>$$\text{level}=v.L=v + \frac{L}{10}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="nonint_scientist_level" class="metric_container">
      <h2>scientist's level (non-integer)</h2>
      <p>The non-integer scientist's level (Todeschini and Baccini 2016) is a logarithmic approach to scaling the total number of citations and publications of an author into a single measure without the degeneracy of the integer measure. It is calculated as</p>$$\tau=\ln{\left(\sqrt{C^P}+P\right)}.$$
      <h3>History</h3>
