    <div id="q_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>q-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>q-</em>index (Bartneck and Kokkelmans 2011) is an attempt to measure whether an author is manipulating their own <em>h-</em>index through self-citation. It calculates a potential cost of a self-citation for each publication whose citation count is equal or less than <em>h,</em> sums these costs for all self-citations, then normalizes by the total number of publications. Essentially, publications with a citation count equal to <em>h</em> generate a cost of 1 per self-citation, with each subsequent publication in rank order generating costs of 1/2, 1/3, 1/4, etc., with ties generating equal values. The potential costs are calculated as:</p>$$q_i=\left|\begin{matrix} 0 & \text{if }C_i > h \text{ or } i < h \\ \frac{1}{i+1-a_i-h} & \text{if }C_i \leq h \text{ and } i \geq h \end{matrix}\right.,$$<p>where</p>$$a_i=\left|\begin{matrix} 0 & \text{if }i \leq h \\ a_{i-1} & \text{if }i>h \text{ and } C_i \neq C_{i-1}\\ 1+a_{i-1} & \text{if }i>h \text{ and } C_i = C_{i-1} \end{matrix}\right. .$$<p>The total self-citations for any publication where <em>C<sub>i</sub></em>&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<em>h</em> are multiplied by the associated cost and these values are summed and averaged for all publications.</p>$$Q=\frac{1}{P}\sum\limits_{i=1}^{P}{c_i q_i}.$$<p>where <em>c<sub>i</sub></em> is the number of self-citations to the <em>i</em><sup>th</sup> publication.</p><p>Simulations indicate that selective citing to boost <em>h</em> may lead to a <em>q</em>-index, greater than one, while lower values are found when self-citation is random or fair.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Self Citations (<em>c<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th><em>a<sub>i</sub></em></th><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td></tr><tr><th><em>q<sub>i</sub></em></th><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>1/2</td><td>1/2</td><td>1/2</td><td>1/3</td><td>1/4</td><td>1/4</td><td>1/4</td><td>1/5</td><td>1/5</td><td>1/5</td></tr><tr><th><em>c<sub>i</sub>q<sub>i</sub></em></th><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>1.500</td><td>1.000</td><td>0.500</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.250</td><td>0.250</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td><td>0.000</td></tr></table><p>The sum of <em>c<sub>i</sub>q<sub>i</sub></em> is 3.5000, thus Q is 0.2188.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="career_years_h_index_by_pub" class="metric_container">
      <h2>career years <em>h-</em>index by publications</h2>
      <p>The career years <em>h-</em>index by publications (Mahbuba and Rousseau 2013) is a measure of publication intensity or distribution, rather than citation intensity as captured by most <em>h-</em>type indices. Rather than create a list of publications ranked by citation count, one creates a list of years ranked by publication count. This list is then processed in the same manner as a typical <em>h-</em>type index, namely the career years <em>h-</em>index by publications is the largest value <em>h</em> for which at least <em>h</em> years have <em>h</em> publications.</p>$$\text{career years }h\text{ by publications}=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i\leq P_i\right).$$<p>This metric helps indicate whether the publication output of a researcher is confined to a limited number of years (smaller value) or is spread more evenly across their career (larger values); it is most useful for comparing among established/older researchers with similar publication counts and career lengths; this metric is limiting for young researchers with short careers because the maximum value is the length of the career.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Years are ordered by number of publications, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Year (<em>y<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>2000</td><td>1997</td><td>2001</td><td>1999</td><td>1998</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Publications (<em>P<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>5</td><td>5</td><td class="box">4</td><td>1</td><td>1</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td class="box">3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em>&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<em>P<sub>i</sub></em> is 3.</p>

    <div id="career_years_h_index_by_cite" class="metric_container">
      <h2>career years <em>h-</em>index by citations</h2>
      <p>The career years <em>h-</em>index by citations (Mahbuba and Rousseau 2013) is a measure of citation intensity or distribution by publication year, rather than by publication as captured by most <em>h-</em>type indices. Rather than create a list of publications ranked by citation count, one creates a list of years ranked by citation count for all publications from that year. This list is then processed in the same manner as a typical <em>h-</em>type index, namely the career years <em>h-</em>index by citations is the largest value <em>h</em> for which at least <em>h</em> years have publications with <em>h</em> total citations.</p>$$\text{career years }h\text{ by citations}=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i\leq P_i\right).$$<p>This metric helps indicate whether the citation impact of a researcher is confined to a limited number of years (smaller value) or is spread more evenly across their career (larger values); it is most useful for comparing among established/older researchers with similar career lengths and citation counts/<em>h-</em>indices; this metric is limiting for young researchers with short careers because the maximum value is the length of the career.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Years are ordered by number of citations for publications from that year, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Year (<em>y<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1997</td><td>2000</td><td>1998</td><td>2001</td><td>1999</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>99</td><td>28</td><td class="box">13</td><td>3</td><td>3</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td class="box">3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em>&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<em>C<sub>i</sub></em> is 3.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

    <div id="career_years_h_index_by_avg_cite" class="metric_container">
      <h2>career years <em>h-</em>index by average citations per year</h2>
      <p>The career years <em>h-</em>index by average citations per year (Mahbuba and Rousseau 2013) is similar to the other two career years <em>h-</em>indices, but is based on the average number of citations generated per publication per year, rather than on the total publications or total citations for each year. One creates a list of years ranked by average citation count per publication for all publications from that year. This list is then processed in the same manner as a typical <em>h-</em>type index, namely the career years <em>h-</em>index by average citations per year is the largest value <em>h</em> for which at least <em>h</em> years have publications with an average of <em>h</em> citations.</p>$$\text{career years }h\text{ by average citations per year}=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i\leq \frac{C_i}{P_i}\right).$$<p>Because the average number of citations will often not be an integer, the authors recommend interpolating between this and the next largest value, as with the real <em>h-</em>index, in order to more accurately estimate the metric.</p>$$h_{int}=\frac{\left(h+1\right)\frac{C_h}{P_h}-h\frac{C_{h+1}}{P_{h+1}}}{1-\frac{C_{h+1}}{P_{h+1}}+\frac{C_h}{P_h}}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Years are ordered by average number of citations per publication from that year, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Year (<em>y<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1997</td><td>1998</td><td>2000</td><td>1999</td><td>2001</td></tr><tr><th>Publications (<em>P<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>5</td><td>1</td><td>5</td><td>1</td><td>4</td></tr><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>99</td><td>13</td><td>28</td><td>3</td><td>3</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Average Citations per Publication(<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>P<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>19.80</td><td>13.00</td><td class="box">5.60</td><td>3.00</td><td>0.75</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td class="box">3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em>&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>P<sub>i</sub></em> is 3. The interpolated rank betweeen this and the next largest value is 3.91.</p>

    <div id="career_years_h_index_by_diff_speed" class="metric_container">
      <h2>career years <em>h-</em>index by diffusion speed</h2>
      <p>The career years <em>h-</em>index by diffusion speed (Mahbuba and Rousseau 2013) is similar to the other career years <em>h-</em>indices, but rescales the citation counts per year for the time ellapsed since the year of publication. One could also calculate it for a fixed time window, but here we are using career length at the time of calcuation. One creates a list of years ranked by diffusion speed estimated for all citations for all publications from that year. This list is then processed in the same manner as a typical <em>h-</em>type index, namely the career years <em>h-</em>index by diffusion speed is the largest value <em>h</em> for which at least <em>h</em> years have a diffusion speed of <em>h</em>.</p>$$\text{career years }h\text{ by diffusion speed}=\underset{i}{\max}\left(i\leq \frac{C_i}{A_i}\right).$$<p>Because the diffusion speed will often not be an integer, the authors recommend interpolating between this and the next largest value, as with the real <em>h-</em>index, in order to more accurately estimate the metric.</p>$$h_{int}=\frac{\left(h+1\right)\frac{C_h}{A_h}-h\frac{C_{h+1}}{A_{h+1}}}{1-\frac{C_{h+1}}{A_{h+1}}+\frac{C_h}{A_h}}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Years are ordered by diffusion speed per year, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Year (<em>y<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>1997</td><td>2000</td><td>1998</td><td>2001</td><td>1999</td></tr><tr><th>Age (<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>5</td><td>2</td><td>4</td><td>1</td><td>3</td></tr><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>99</td><td>28</td><td>13</td><td>3</td><td>3</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Diffusion Speed (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>19.80</td><td>14.00</td><td class="box">3.25</td><td>3.00</td><td>1.00</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td class="box">3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;3</td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The largest rank where <em>i</em>&nbsp;≤&nbsp;<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>/<em>A<sub>i</sub></em> is 3. The interpolated rank betweeen this and the next largest value is 3.20.</p>

    <div id="collaborative_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>collaborative index</h2>
      <p>The Collaborative Index (Lawani 1980) is a basic measurement of the degree of collaboration within a set of publications. It is calculated as:</p>$$\text{CI}=\frac{\sum\limits_{a=1}^{a_\max}{af_a}}{P},$$<p>where <em>f<sub>a</sub></em> is the number of publications having <em>a</em> authors and <em>a</em><sub>max</sub> is the largest number of authors for any publication.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <table class="example_table"><tr><th># of Authors (<em>a</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th># of Publications (<em>f<sub>a</sub></em>)</th><td>5</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><th>Product (<em>a&times;f<sub>a</sub></em>)</th><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>9</td><td>20</td></tr></table><p>The sum of the products is 40, which yields a collaborative index of 2.5 when divided by the total number of publications (16).</p>
      <h3>History</h3>

        <div class="table_container">

    <div id="degree_of_collaboration" class="metric_container">
      <h2>degree of collaboration</h2>
      <p>The Degree of Collaboration index (Subramanyam 1983) is simply the proportion of a researcher's publications which are coauthored,</p>$$\text{DC}=1-\frac{f_1}{P},$$<p>where <em>f</em><sub>1</sub> is the number of single-authored publications.
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th>DC</th></tr>

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">0.7500</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">0.7407</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">0.6562</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">0.6667</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2007</td><td class="cell_value">0.6944</td></tr>

@@
    <div id="collaborative_coefficient" class="metric_container">
      <h2>collaborative coefficient</h2>
      <p>The Collaborative Coefficient (Ajiferuke <em>et al.</em> 1988) is another measure of the degree of collaboration within a set of publications. It is calculated as:</p>$$\text{CC}=1-\frac{\sum\limits_{a=1}^{a_\max}{\frac{f_a}{a}}}{P},$$<p>where <em>f<sub>a</sub></em> is the number of publications having <em>a</em> authors and <em>a</em><sub>max</sub> is the largest number of authors for any publication.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <table class="example_table"><tr><th># of Authors (<em>a</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th># of Publications (<em>f<sub>a</sub></em>)</th><td>5</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>5</td></tr><tr><th>Quotient (<em>f<sub>a</sub></em>/<em>a</em>)</th><td>5.00</td><td>1.50</td><td>1.00</td><td>1.25</td></tr></table><p>The sum of the quotients is 8.75, which yields a collaborative index of 0.4531.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">
            <tr><th>Year</th><th>CC</th></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1997</td><td class="cell_value">0.5667</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1998</td><td class="cell_value">0.5556</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">1999</td><td class="cell_value">0.5833</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2000</td><td class="cell_value">0.5208</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2001</td><td class="cell_value">0.4531</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2002</td><td class="cell_value">0.4915</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2003</td><td class="cell_value">0.5169</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2004</td><td class="cell_value">0.5150</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2005</td><td class="cell_value">0.4554</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2006</td><td class="cell_value">0.4567</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2007</td><td class="cell_value">0.4650</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2008</td><td class="cell_value">0.4791</td></tr>

            <tr><td class="cell_year">2016</td><td class="cell_value">0.5167</td></tr>
            <tr><td class="cell_year">2017</td><td class="cell_value">0.5221</td></tr>

    <div id="apparent_h_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2>apparent <em>h-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The apparent <em>h-</em>index (Mohammed <em>et al.</em> 2020) is simply the <em>h-</em>index times the proportion of publications that have at least one citation; it is similar to the normalized <em>h-</em>index.</p>$$h_A=h \frac{P_{\textrm{non-zero cites}}}{P}.$$
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The <em>h-</em>index is 6. Of the 16 publications, 13 have at least one citation. The apparent <em>h-</em>index is therefore 6&times;13/16 = 4.875</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
      <div class="metric_data_container">
        <div class="table_container">
          <table class="impact_table">

    <div id="chi_index" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>χ-</em>index</h2>
      <p>The <em>χ-</em>index (Fenner <em>et al.</em> 2018) is the square-root of the size of the largest rectangle which can fit under the citation curve; similar to how <em>h</em> is the size of the largest square. It is the square-root of the <em>rec-</em>index.</p><div id="chart_chi_index_desc_div" class="proportional_chart"></div>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th><em>i</em>&times;<em>C<sub>i</sub></em></th><td>49</td><td>54</td><td class="box">69</td><td>52</td><td>50</td><td>60</td><td>21</td><td>24</td><td>27</td><td>20</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr></table><p>The largest rectangle which can fit under the citation curve has an area of 69, the square-root of which is <em>χ</em> = 8.307.</p>

    <div id="reci_recp" class="metric_container">
      <h2><em>rec<sub>I</sub></em>, <em>rec<sub>P</sub></em></h2>
      <p>The [<em>rec<sub>I</sub></em>, <em>rec<sub>P</sub></em>] metrics (Levene <em>et al.</em> 2020) are a two-dimensional expansion of the <em>rec</em>-index, where <em>rec<sub>I</sub></em> is the largest vertical (influential) rectangle that can fit under the citation curve and <em>rec<sub>P</sub></em> is the largest horizontal (prolific) rectangle that can fit under the citation curve. The minimum size of both is the area of the <em>h-</em>square; the larger of the two is equal to the <em>rec</em>-index.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td>10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td>6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th><em>i</em>&times;<em>C<sub>i</sub></em></th><td>49</td><td>54</td><td class="box">69</td><td>52</td><td>50</td><td>60</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr class="top_row"><th></th><td></td><td></td><td><em>rec<sub>I</sub></em></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><th>min(<em>h</em>, <em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td>6</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>min(<em>h</em>, <em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)&times;<em>i</em></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td class="box">36</td><td>21</td><td>24</td><td>27</td><td>20</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td> <em>rec<sub>P</sub></em></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table>

    <div id="academic_trace" class="metric_container">
      <h2>academic trace</h2>
      <p>The Academic Trace (Ye and Leydesdorff 2014; Ye <em>et al.</em> 2017) starts by taking the concept of the citation curve and describing it as three vectors of information. The first vector (<strong>X</strong>) separates publications into those in the core (X<sub>1</sub>), those in the tail with at least one citation (X<sub>2</sub>), and those with zero citations (X<sub>3</sub>). These three values are each calculated as the square of the count of publications in each category, divided by the total number of publications. The second vector (<strong>Y</strong>) separates citations into those in the core block (Y<sub>1</sub>), those in the tail (Y<sub>2</sub>), and the excess citations in the core above the core block (Y<sub>3</sub>). These values are calculated as the square of the count of citations within each category, divided by the total citation. The third vector (<strong>Z</strong>) contrasts particularly highly cited publications and those with zero citations, and is simply the difference of the corresponding values of the first two vectors, X<sub>1</sub> - Y<sub>1</sub>, X<sub>2</sub> - Y<sub>2</sub>, and X<sub>3</sub> - Y<sub>3</sub>. These vectors form a 3D matrix, the trace of which is a measure of academic performance. The trace can be determined as X<sub>1</sub> + Y<sub>2</sub> + Z<sub>3</sub>; a "shortcut" formula (Ding <em>et al.</em> 2020) is as follows:</p><p>$$tr\left(V\right)=\frac{h^4+\left(C^h-h^2\right)^2}{C^P} + \frac{\left( P-h-P_z\right)^2-P_z^2}{P}.$$</p><p>where <em>P<sub>z</sub></em> is the number of publications with zero citations and the rest of the values have been defined earlier.</p>
      <h3>Example</h3>
      <p>Publications are ordered by number of citations, from highest to lowest.</p><table class="example_table"><tr class="top_row"><th>Citations (<em>C<sub>i</sub></em>)</th><td>49</td><td>27</td><td>23</td><td>13</td><td>10</td><td class="box">10</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>3</td><td>2</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>1</td><td>0</td><td>0</td><td>0</td></tr><tr><th>Rank (<em>i</em>)</th><td>1</td><td>2</td><td>3</td><td>4</td><td>5</td><td class="box">6</td><td>7</td><td>8</td><td>9</td><td>10</td><td>11</td><td>12</td><td>13</td><td>14</td><td>15</td><td>16</td></tr><tr><th></th><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td><em>h</em>&nbsp;=&nbsp;6</td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr></table><p>The <em>h</em>-index is 6; the total number of citations (<em>C<sup>P</sup></em>) is 146; the number of citations in the top <em>h</em> publications (<em>C<sup>h</sup></em>) is 132; the total number of publications (P) is 16; and the number of publications with zero citations (<em>P<sub>z</sub></em>) is 3. Entered in the above formula, the academic trace is 74.5000.</p>
      <h3>History</h3>
